Background on the problem domain and influential algorithms

#./chpt1.tex#

Introduction

Introduction

In the technology age, life is almost unfathomable without the mobile phone. It is hard to believe how the business world managed to function in the pre-mobile phone era1.1.

The invention of the mobile phone fulfilled the need to always be connected and be within reach of the modern world. This need can actually be attributed to feeling part of something and this something is deemed as being part of a network. This is similar to how mobile phones are able to provide connectivity at almost any location within a country.

Behind the connectivity of mobile phones lies an intricate layer of communication technology which has evolved since the invention of the normal landline phone. This technology is referred to at the top level as wireless communication, which includes inventions such as the radio. To enable communication at the level needed by mobile phones, wireless communication had to evolve and go a step further than normal radio communication. Hence the concept of cellular mobile networks was developed.

Without cellular networks mobile phones have no means of providing communication. The purpose of the cellular network is to provide the necessary functions to connect and facilitate communication between two entities (mobile phones) over a wireless network.

Cellular networks achieve this level of communication through expensive equipment and ``smart'' algorithms. By ``smart'' algorithms, what is actually meant is algorithms that utilise artificial intelligence concepts to perform a certain function in an attempt to either automate a task within the network or improve a certain part of the network. Therefore, the correct term for these types of algorithms is actually artificial intelligence (AI) algorithms.

AI algorithms have a wide spread of functions that they can perform ranging from making informed intelligent decisions to optimising the operation of certain processes. In particular computers are indeed very apt at optimising certain procedures. This is because a computer is able to test and evaluate a huge number of different alterations and combinations of a certain procedure in a short amount of time, thereby allowing it to find the best combination out of those tested.

In this dissertation an algorithm is presented which concentrates on cellular phone networks. This particular algorithm falls into the latter part of the AI algorithms discussed, namely an AI optimisation algorithm. Thus the algorithm presented in this dissertation operates on a cellular phone network to optimise a certain part of the network.

In this section a introduction was presented to the dissertation. The main themes of the dissertation were outlined namely, cellular phone networks and optimisation algorithms. In the next section research plan and chapter breakdown is presented which is followed by this dissertation.

Research Questions and Objective

As previously discussed, an artificial intelligent algorithm is presented in this dissertation that generates plans for use by cellular networks. The purpose of creating such an algorithm is to determine the applicability of modern swarm-based algorithms with regard to finding better solutions to real-world problems that exist in domains such as cellular communication.

The research approach followed in this dissertation was first and foremost to understand the cellular network domain. More importantly, the focus is on how and what frequencies are used to facilitate communication as well as what affects the quality of the communicational link between two entities in a cellular network.

The cellular domain is the problem domain, but to develop a solution in the problem domain a study needs to be conducted in the artificial intelligence domain, namely a study of optimisation algorithms. In order for a new optimisation algorithm to be developed, other algorithms that have achieved success in the respective optimisation problem domains in which they have been applied need to be investigated.

Therefore in an attempt to develop and apply a modern viable optimisation-based algorithm, a series of research questions has been identified which need to be answered. The research questions are as follows:

  • Question 1 --- What is cellular technology, how it got developed and what improvements have been made since its initial development?
  • Question 2 --- What is the architecture behind a modern cellular network, how do the various hardware entities within a network communicate with each other and how is a communicational link established between two users of the network?
  • Question 3 --- What exactly is the frequency problem and how does it affect modern wireless communication?
  • Question 4 --- What variants of the frequency problem exist and which are most applicable to cellular networks?
  • Question 5 --- What are the most popular optimisation algorithms and what characteristics make them unique?
  • Question 6 --- With algorithms that achieved success in their respective optimisation problems, what particular technique is used by the algorithms that allowed them to achieve better performance?

The PSO algorithm might be short and elegant, but applying it to the FS-FAP required various new techniques in response to the following questions:

  • How can a particle best be represented as a frequency plan?
  • How can one frequency plan move to another?
  • How can particles be prevented from using forbidden frequencies when they fly towards a particular plan?

The above questions were only the preliminary questions and were in fact a problem that had to be addressed for successful application of the PSO to the FAP. In chapter~#chpt:psoapplicationFAP#350> a discussion is presented on how these problems were solved as well as how other problems were solved that were not anticipated.

Throughout the course of this dissertation the aim is to answer each of these identified research questions. In the next section a chapter breakdown of this dissertation is presented.

The aim of this dissertation is first and foremost to answer the identified research questions. Answering these questions will aid in reaching the research objective of this dissertation. The research objective is therefore to develop and determine the viability of an algorithm based on modern swarm optimisation techniques that will operate on the frequency assignment problem in an attempt to provide better frequency plans for use in cellular networks.

Chapter Breakdown

Part I - Background

The first part of this dissertation is concerned with the domain and problem the algorithm presented in this dissertation addresses, and finally to also understand the intricate details of how the algorithm operates.

Below is an outline of the chapters in the first part of this dissertation.

Chapter 1

This chapter provides an introduction to the dissertation as well as a broad overview of the topics in the dissertation will discuss.

Chapter 2

This chapter is concerned with providing information on how a modern cellular network functions. Within this chapter a brief history is presented on how cellular network technology was developed. The chapter also provides an overview of the architecture of a cellular network,and each part of the network's intended purpose and function to facilitate wireless communication is discussed.

Chapter 3

This chapter presents the problem that the dissertation addresses, namely the frequency assignment problem. The chapter provides a discussion on why the problem exists, the causes of the problem and what it means for a problem to be NP-Complete. Furthermore the variants of the problem and how they differ depending on the wireless domain that is being considered are alos discussed. Finally the chapter also provides a formal definition of the problem which is later utilised by the algorithm developed in this research.

Chapter 4

This chapter marks the beginning of a discussion on various optimisation algorithms in this dissertation. The algorithms presented in this chapter were chosen due to their widespread usage as well as success on NP-Complete problems. Each algorithm is discussed in depth providing an outline of the core features that make the algorithm unique as well as each core feature in detail. For each algorithm the chapter also presents an analysis on related work of the particular algorithm being applied to the frequency assignment problem.

Chapter 5

This chapter is concerned with providing algorithms that are new in the research domain of optimisation algorithms. The algorithms presented in chapter 4 are fairly old and have been applied to a wide variety of problems. Algorithms in this chapter are relatively new in the optimisation domain and have not been applied to the same number of problems as the algorithms in chapter 4. In this chapter swarm algorithms are presented and the algorithms have the particular characteristic that they are based generally on processes observed in nature. Each algorithm is discussed in depth with its core characteristics outlined. Furthermore for each algorithm an analysis is given if the algorithm were to be applied to the frequency assignment problem. Finally it is formally stated what algorithm this dissertation is applied to the frequency assignment problem in this research.

Part II - Implementation

Chapter 6

This chapter provides a discussion of the algorithm developed to be applied to the frequency assignment problem. Within this chapter an outline is given of the process in developing a specialised particle swarm algorithm for the frequency algorithm. Each specialised technique developed is discussed in depth, along with an explanation of why the technique is needed as well as why it is used by the algorithm.

Chapter 7

This chapter is concerned with providing the results after applying the algorithm to a specialised set of benchmark problems for frequency assignment algorithms. The particular selected benchmark problems were discussed in chapter 2.

Chapter 8

This chapter concludes this dissertation. In this chapter it is determined whether the research goal was reached as well as whether any future work can be done to improve the presented algorithm. #./chpt1.tex#


Cellular Communication Technology

Introduction

Wireless technology is used by modern electronic devices to establish communication over which information is exchanged[#Karen2004##1###]. Wireless technology facilitates communication between two devices by transmitting data or voice via a radio frequency[#Karen2004##1###]. Examples of devices exchanging information wirelessly are radios for audio entertainment; television remotes to change channels; cellular phones for communication; wireless access points to create wireless LANs[#Karen2004##1###].

The popularity and rapid adoption of wireless technology contributes to the difficulty of planning, managing and operating wireless networks[#Karen2004##1###]. As the popularity and use of services that use wireless technology increase, the need for these services to use different radio frequencies to communicate becomes greater[#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###]. This need arises due to an effect called interference, which occurs when two or more connections between devices use the same radio frequency to facilitate communication[#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###]. This effect is discussed in more detail in chapter 3.

A wireless cellular operator is not allowed to operate on just any frequency. A governing body licenses a certain piece of the available wireless spectrum to the operator for use in their network[#FAPRAMColouring##1###]. These frequencies that need to be licensed for use are known as commercial frequencies and are very scarce as it is immensely expensive to license[#FAPRAMColouring##1###].

The use of frequencies by services must be carefully considered to avoid interference when devices communicate with each other. This is a problem as the amount of devices that communicate far outstrips the amount of frequencies available for communication[#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###]. Thus assigning frequencies to devices for communication is a difficult problem and is referred to as the <#410#>FAP<#410#>[#Karen2004##1###,#Eisenblatter##1###].

Initially manual techniques were used to assign frequencies in an attempt to solve the <#412#>FAP<#412#>[#Karen2004##1###]. As a result, assigning frequencies was either too complex or just too daunting because of the sheer number of devices that needed to be assigned frequencies[#Karen2004##1###]. Also, because of the rapid adoption of wireless technology the assignment of frequencies needed to be dynamic and hence automated[#Karen2004##1###].

By understanding how a <#416#>gsm<#416#> network operates, especially how communication is facilitated, the reader is able to understand the problem this dissertation addresses. Therefore, this chapter will start off with section~#sec:GSMNet#417> within which a brief history of <#418#>gsm<#418#> networks are presented. Section~#sec:GSMArch#419> follows the history discussion and presents an overview on the different architectural components the form a <#420#>gsm<#420#> network. In section~#sec:gsminterfaces#421> the different interfaces used by <#422#>gsm<#422#> network components for communication is presented. Section~#sec:interfacech#423> presents an overview on the logical channels that frequencies get divided into when used in a <#424#>gsm<#424#> network. Finally this chapter concludes with a discussion on the handover process in section~#sec:handover#425>.


GSM Networks

The <#428#>gsm<#428#> is a system for multiservice cellular communication that is capable of providing voice as well as data services[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###,#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###]. Most cellular networks in operation are <#430#>gsm<#430#> based[#Karen2004##1###,#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###]. The primary service that <#432#>gsm<#432#> caters for is voice communication, but other data services such as <#433#>SMS<#433#>, <#434#>MMS<#434#> and internet connectivity services, e.g. <#435#>GPRS<#435#>, <#436#>EDGE<#436#> and <#437#>HSCSD<#437#>, are becoming more important[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###,#Eisenblatter##1###].

<#439#>gsm<#439#> is one of the most widely used radio communication technologies, which is why one needs to look at the history behind it in order to understand the domain of radio communication better [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. A brief history of the <#441#>gsm<#441#> network specification will now be presented in the next section.


A Brief History of GSM Networks

In early 1981 a group known as the Groupe Speciale Mobile was established to develop a Europe-wide radio communication system using the reserved 900 MHz band2.1

At the start of the <#448#>gsm<#448#> specification in the early 1980s it was initially thought that the system would be analogue based, but this soon changed with the <#449#>ISDN<#449#> specification nearing completion[#GSM92##1###]. As such the <#451#>gsm<#451#> specification started following many of the same design principles and access protocols that <#452#>ISDN<#452#> exhibited[#GSM92##1###].

After the completion of the <#454#>ISDN<#454#> specification, the advantages of switching to digital instead of analogue for communication became clear[#GSM92##1###] One of the primary advantages of <#456#>ISDN<#456#> is that it is capable of transmitting data at higher speeds[#GSM92##1###] <#458#>gsm<#458#> would therefore be based on digital transmission and speech would be represented by a digital stream of 16 kbits/s [#GSM92##1###].

Before the switch to digital transmission was finalised the Groupe Speciale Mobile first wanted to evaluate the spectral efficiency of analogue and digital-based transmission[#GSM92##1###]. Spectral efficiency plays an important part in wireless communication since the radio spectrum is a limited resource and whichever transmission technology is used, the utilisation of the spectrum should be maximised[#GSM92##1###].

Maximum utilisation is an important problem that is discussed in detail in later sections of this chapter. After an evaluation of spectral efficiency it was decided that the <#462#>gsm<#462#> system would be digitally based using <#463#>TDMA<#463#> [#GSM92##1###,#GSMSysEngin##1###]. <#465#>TDMA<#465#> is discussed in section~#sec:interfacech#466>.

By the early 1990’s <#467#>gsm<#467#> became an evolving standard and the first <#468#>gsm<#468#>-based network was demonstrated in 19912.2[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###,#Eisenblatter##1###]. The following year a number of <#472#>gsm<#472#> networks were operating in Europe due to mobile terminals and equipment capable of operating on the networks becoming more widely available to the general public[#GSM92##1###,#Eisenblatter##1###]. In the same year an operator in Australia became the first non-European operator to implement a <#474#>gsm<#474#>-based network[#Eisenblatter##1###].

The collective subscriber base of <#476#>gsm<#476#> networks surpassed the million subscriber mark in 1993[#GSM92##1###]. Due to this phenomenal growth in <#478#>gsm<#478#> network use, numerous extensions were made to the <#479#>gsm<#479#> specification. Some of the extensions that were made are the following[#GSM92##1###,#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]:

  • Half rate speech codec
  • Improved SMS
  • Line identification
  • Call waiting
  • Call holding
The specification with these extensions is known as <#483#>gsm<#483#> Phase 2+. As the world shifted towards more digital and data-intensive services it became difficult to deliver these services over <#484#>gsm<#484#> networks. This difficulty was due to the restriction that data could only be transmitted at 9.6 kbps [#GSM92##1###,#Karen2004##1###].

This restriction also applies to voice, as voice is encoded into data that is transmitted over the network[#Karen2004##1###,#GSM92##1###]. Before the extensions were made, voice was encoded using the Full Rate codec[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. The Full rate codec was the first codec used to encode voice in the <#488#>gsm<#488#> standard and required the full 9.6 kpbs that was available[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. With half rate speech telephony, only half of the 9.6 kpbs is required for the same voice quality to be delivered[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

The new specification defined new technologies such as GPRS, EDGE and HSCSD, which were designed with the primary goal of making more bandwidth available for data transmission [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###,#Karen2004##1###]. Data transmission was improved by these technologies by enabling more than one <#492#>gsm<#492#> slot to be used for a terminal or service at a time[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###,#Karen2004##1###].

If more than one <#494#>gsm<#494#> slot is to be used by a terminal or service, transceivers are required to have a higher <#495#>SIR<#495#> ratio [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###,#GSMSysEngin##1###]. This requirement affects radio interfaces as there is a higher likelihood that interference might occur; hence it makes it more difficult to generate a low interference frequency plan [#Eisenblatter##1###,#GSMSysEngin##1###].

The actual <#498#>SIR<#498#> at a receiver is dependent on a number of factors that include [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###,#Karen2004##1###]:

  • Frequency used at the transceiver
  • Strength of the signal
  • Weather conditions
  • Shape of the surrounding environment
  • Direction of the transmission
Even taking these factors into account, the calculation of the <#502#>SIR<#502#> at a transceiver is not trivial. This calculation of the <#503#>SIR<#503#> as well as its impact on mobile radio frequencies is discussed in section~#sec:Interference#504>.

As the <#505#>gsm<#505#> standard matured as a cellular technology, industry experts began specification of the next generation of cellular networks, which would in time replace the <#506#>gsm<#506#> cellular system.

The <#507#>UMTS<#507#> is considered the third generation (3G) of cellular networks[#tabuglobalplanning3g##1###,#Eisenblatter##1###]. <#509#>UMTS<#509#> was designed from the beginning to operate in parallel with the legacy <#510#>gsm<#510#> system. The first standard of <#511#>UMTS<#511#> was issued in the beginning of 2000 and subsequently most modern networks are based on it or are migrating their networks to it[#tabuglobalplanning3g##1###,#Eisenblatter##1###].

UMTS is a major improvement over the <#513#>gsm<#513#> in two areas, namely data transmission bandwidth and frequency planning due to <#514#>UMTS<#514#> utilising <#515#>DS-CDMA<#515#> and <#516#>WCDMA<#516#>[#tabuglobalplanning3g##1###,#Eisenblatter##1###]. The higher data transmission speed (2 Mb/s) can be attributed to <#518#>UMTS<#518#> using the <#519#>DS-CDMA<#519#> scheme[#tabuglobalplanning3g##1###,#Eisenblatter##1###]. The scheme also allows more users to be served than previous generations of networks[#tabuglobalplanning3g##1###,#Eisenblatter##1###].

DS-CDMA and <#522#>WCDMA<#522#> sends data over a wide band spectrum of 5 MHz. The wider spectrum allows for more frequencies to be utilised and with more frequencies being available; the <#523#>FAP<#523#> is easier to solve compared to a <#524#>gsm<#524#> network[#tabuglobalplanning3g##1###,#Eisenblatter##1###]. A more in depth discussion on how a range of spectrum is divided into frequencies for communication in <#526#>gsm<#526#> networks is discussed in section~#sec:interfacech#527>. <#528#>DS-CDMA<#528#> and <#529#>WCDMA<#529#> will not be discussed in depth as this discussion focusses on <#530#>gsm<#530#>. The interested reader is directed to [#tabuglobalplanning3g##1###] and [#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###] for more information.

<#533#>CDMA<#533#> is a technology primarily used in broadband systems[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. Users do not gain access to only a small portion of the bandwidth, but rather use the entire band for the duration of a connection[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. Users also do not gain exclusive access to the whole band, but instead share the usage of the bandwidth with other users simultaneously, hence the name multiple access[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

With <#538#>CDMA<#538#> a user's signal is not transmitted as its original signal. Instead the signal is spectrally spread over a multiple of its original bandwidth using a spreading factor [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. The spreading factor fluctuates between values of 10 and 1 000 [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. Using these spreading factors less interference and fewer disturbances are encountered because the broadband signal is generated from a narrowband signal [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. UMTS may be a major improvement, but its adoption does not spread very far from busy city centers where there is a large concentration of clients in a small geographical area[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. The reason for this is that, as mentioned previously, <#543#>UMTS<#543#> caters for larger data usage and therefore more clients can be serviced simultaneously [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

Most network operators do not implement entirely new backbone architecture for <#545#>UMTS<#545#> to operate on, but instead utilise the same backbone used for <#547#>gsm<#547#> and GPRS[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. This not only extends the lifetime of previous infrastructure investment by the operator, but also builds upon the redundancy provided by the <#549#>gsm<#549#> network [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. Thus even with new technological improvements such as <#551#>UMTS<#551#>, <#552#>gsm<#552#> as a wireless technology is still used for communication and is therefore still relevant today[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

The most up-to-date cellular network technology is referred to as the fourth generation of cellular networks (4G)[#4GWirelessVid##1###]. The purpose of 4G is to improve upon 3G and bring true broadband speeds that is delivered over the cellular network[#4GWirelessVid##1###]. There are currently two competing technologies that aim to provide 4G capabilities to cellular networks which is <#556#>LTE<#556#> and Mobile <#557#>WIMAX<#557#>[#4GWirelessVid##1###]. Both technologies provide broadband speeds in the range of 14 - 40 MBits/s[#4GWirelessVid##1###]. According to the 4G standard both technologies cannot be considered as ``true'' 4G technologies as the standard defines that broadband speeds in excess of 100 Mbits/s must be provided to be considered true <#560#>LTE<#560#>[#4GWirelessVid##1###].

In this section a brief overview on the history of the <#562#>gsm<#562#> network was presented. In the next section an explanation of the topology of <#563#>gsm<#563#> network is given. This will broaden the understanding of <#564#>gsm<#564#> networks.


Topology of a GSM Network

A <#567#>gsm<#567#> network consists of a variety of different subsystems to realise the goal of establishing a radio communication link between two parties. The hierarchy of systems and their respective connections to each other are illustrated in figure~#fig:GSMArchitecture#568>. Each subsystem will now be discussed.

Mobile Station

A <#570#>MS<#570#> as it is defined in the <#571#>gsm<#571#> specification refers to any mobile device that is capable of making and receiving calls on a <#572#>gsm<#572#> network. The <#573#>MS<#573#> is the main gateway for a user to gain access to the <#574#>gsm<#574#> network [#Eisenblatter##1###,#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. Typical devices that fall under the category of <#576#>MS<#576#> are cellular phones, smart phones and <#577#>POS<#577#> devices. The <#578#>MS<#578#> has two features that play an important role throughout the <#579#>gsm<#579#> network, namely:
<#14868#><#581#>SIM<#581#><#14868#>
--- Inserted into a mobile device. The <#582#>SIM<#582#> contains the <#583#>IMSI<#583#> and is used throughout the network for authentication as well as being a key part in providing encrypted transmissions [#Eisenblatter##1###].
<#14869#><#585#>IMEI<#585#><#14869#>
--- Used to identify mobile station equipment. Primarily used in the denial of service to equipment that has been blacklisted2.3 and tries to gain access to the network [#Eisenblatter##1###].
<#14872#>Figure<#14872#> 2.1: <#14873#>GSM architecture[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]<#14873#>
#figure589#
The <#688#>MS<#688#> has the capability to change the transmission power it uses from 0.8, 2, 5, 8 MW to a maximum value of 20 MW[#GSMSysEngin##1###]. The change in transmission power is automatically set to the lowest level by the <#690#>BTS<#690#> to ensure reliable communication after evaluating the signal strength as measured by the <#691#>MS<#691#> [#GSMSysEngin##1###,#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. The power adjustment also minimises co-channel interference because the transmitted signal is less powerful and therefore less likely to interfere with other signals[#GSMSysEngin##1###]. Co-channel interference is discussed in chapter~#chpt:fap#694>

Base Station Subsystem

According to the <#696#>gsm<#696#> Phase 2+ specification, this system is viewed by the <#697#>MSC<#697#> through an Abis radio interface. The <#698#>BSS<#698#> is responsible for communicating with mobile stations in a particular location area [#Eisenblatter##1###]. The <#700#>BSS<#700#> usually consists of one <#701#>BSC<#701#> with one or more <#702#>BTS<#702#> that it controls [#Eisenblatter##1###].A <#704#>BTS<#704#> has similar equipment to that of a MS[#GSMSysEngin##1###]. Both have transceivers, antennae and the necessary functions to perform radio communication.

The communication link between the <#706#>MSC<#706#> and <#707#>BSC<#707#> is the called the A-interface. The interface between the <#708#>BSC<#708#> and <#709#>BTS<#709#> is called the Abis interface [#Eisenblatter##1###]. The A and Abis interface as well as other interfaces between other <#711#>gsm<#711#> architecture components are discussed in section~#sec:gsminterfaces#712>.

In a <#713#>gsm<#713#> network a service area is defined as a geographical area where the networks wants to deliver its cellular network service to potential clients [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###,#Karen2004##1###]. The service area is subdivided into <#715#>LA<#715#> which are then further divided into smaller radio zones called cells [#GSMSecurInTeleNetwork##1###]. When a cellular network is modelled, cells are modelled as hexagonal shapes. Each cell in the modelled network is served by only one <#717#>BTS<#717#> and is usually regarded to be in the centre of a cell as can be seen in figure~#fig:GSMCell#718>[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

<#14876#>Figure<#14876#> 2.2: <#14877#>Cells with BTSs<#14877#>
#figure720#
Even though cells are modelled as being hexagons (see figure~#fig:GSMCell#754>), the actual coverage area of a cell has no predefined regular shape [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

For the purpose of this research a <#756#>BTS<#756#> is considered to be assigned channels which are used for communication. No discussion will be presented on the electro-magnetic wavelength properties of channels as it has no effect on the results presented or problem being investigated.

With the network modelled as a series of interconnecting hexagons it allows one to more easily take constraints into account[#Eisenblatter##1###]. For a cell to serve its geographical area, it needs to be allocated frequencies to operate on. Therefore, for each cell #tex2html_wrap_inline14880# mathend000# in the modelled network a subset #tex2html_wrap_inline14882# mathend000# of frequencies from the total frequencies #tex2html_wrap_inline14884# mathend000# allocated to the <#758#>gsm<#758#> network is assigned[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. Neighbouring cells must at all costs avoid having the same subset of frequencies allocated to them, since such a scenario would lead to severe interference on any communication and thus degrade quality [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

Since the number of cells in a network greatly outnumber the available of subset frequencies available, one is forced to start reusing frequency subsets in cells[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. To ensure that the reused frequency subsets do not interfere with their neighbouring cells, a reuse distance #tex2html_wrap_inline14886# mathend000# is defined [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. The reuse distance means that a certain number of cells must be between the cell already assigned the frequency subset #tex2html_wrap_inline14888# mathend000# and the cell to be assigned a frequency subset [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. The amount of cells is the distance value #tex2html_wrap_inline14890# mathend000#.

The size of a cell determines the amount of potential traffic that the cell will be required to handle [#GSM92##1###,#Eisenblatter##1###,#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. Therefore, if the size of a cell is chosen to be small, fewer channels will be required to be allocated to that cell as it would not be required to handle as much traffic as a larger cell[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

By making the size of cells smaller, the network operator is required to invest more into its network infrastructure. Smaller cells have a direct consequence that more cells would be required to serve the same geographical area, and more cells means that more <#766#>BTS<#766#> etc. need to be built and maintained, more locations need to be rented [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. Hence, making a cell smaller has a compounding effect on the amount of infrastructure needed to support it.

Fortunately all the extra infrastructure investment by the operator can be greatly scaled down if cells are divided into sectors. Each sector performs the exact same function as a traditional cell and is therefore regarded to also be a cell, just smaller in size and not omnidirectional [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###,#GSM92##1###,#GSMSysEngin##1###].

A cell is divided into 3 to 6 service sectors and each sector is allocated an antenna/transceiver [#GSMSysEngin##1###]. Figure~#fig:cellsector#771> illustrates a cell divided into sectors.

<#14891#>Figure<#14891#> 2.3: <#14892#>Cell sectorisation[#GSMSysEngin##1###].<#14892#>
#tex2html_wrap14894#

Depending on how many sectors are at a cell, the operating angles of the antennae need to be adjusted accordingly to ensure 360 degree service. If there is only one sector, an omnidirectional antenna is used, otherwise the antennae operating angles are adjusted to #math48##tex2html_wrap_inline14896# mathend000# where #tex2html_wrap_inline14898# mathend000# is the number of antennae [#Eisenblatter##1###].

By dividing the cell into sectors the amount of co-channel interference that would occur in a cell is greatly reduced [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. It is important to note that the reduction of co-channel interference is only applicable when the angle of the antenna by which transmission occurs is restricted[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

Suppose, if a cell using an omnidirectional transceiver is assigned 6 channels. If the cell were to be divided into three sectors, where the sectors' antennae are #tex2html_wrap_inline14900# mathend000# apart, the number of interfering co-channels shrinks from 6 to 2 and from 6 to one in the case when the cell is divided into 6 sectors [#GSMSysEngin##1###,#GSM92##1###,#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

Each sector operates one or more elementary transceivers called <#786#>TRX<#786#>. The number of <#787#>TRX<#787#> per sector is determined by the expected peak traffic demand that the cell must be able to handle. Each <#788#>TRX<#788#> can handle 7 to 8 communication links or calls in parallel except the first <#789#>TRX<#789#>, which handles fewer calls than normal because it is responsible for transmitting cell organisation and protocol information [#Eisenblatter##1###]. <#791#>TRX<#791#> are able to handle 7-8 calls in parallel due to the use of <#792#>FDM<#792#> and <#793#>TDM<#793#> schemes.

<#794#>TRX<#794#> are assigned channels, which enable them to provide conversion between digital traffic data on the network side and the radio communication between <#795#>MS<#795#>and the <#796#>gsm<#796#> network[#ACOvsEA##1###,#FAPOrientationModel##1###]. The channels that are used by a cell for communication are discussed in section~#sec:interfacech#798>.

Mobile Switching Centre

The <#800#>MSC<#800#> is at the heart of a cellular switching system and forms part of the <#801#>NSS<#801#>. The <#802#>MSC<#802#> is responsible for the setting up, routing and supervision of calls between <#803#>gsm<#803#> subscribers[#GSM92##1###,#GSMSysEngin##1###]. The <#805#>MSC<#805#> has interfaces to communicate with <#806#>gsm<#806#> subscribers (through the <#807#>BSS<#807#>) on the one hand and with external networks on the other[#GSM92##1###]. The <#809#>MSC<#809#> interfaces with external networks to utilise their superior capability in data transmission as well as for the signalling and communication with other <#810#>gsm<#810#> network components [#GSM92##1###].

The most basic functions that an <#812#>MSC<#812#> is responsible for in a network are the following [#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###]:

  • Voice call initialization, routing, control and supervision between subscribers
  • Handover process between two cells
  • Location updating
  • <#815#>MS<#815#> authentication
  • <#816#>SMS<#816#> delivery
  • Charging and accounting of services used by subscriber
  • Notification of other network elements
  • Administration input or output processing functions

To achieve most of these functions the <#818#>MSC<#818#> has an integrated <#819#>VLR<#819#> database that stores call setup information for any <#820#>MS<#820#> that is currently registered for service with the <#821#>MSC<#821#> [#GSM92##1###,#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###].

The <#823#>VLR<#823#>retrieves this information from the <#824#>HLR<#824#> that contains all the registered <#825#>gsm<#825#> subscriber information for the network. This information enables the <#826#>MSC<#826#> to quickly retrieve the necessary information to set up a call between two clients that want to communicate with each other [#GSMSysEngin##1###,#GSMSecurInTeleNetwork##1###].

A requirement for being able to communicate with other network elements such as <#828#>PSTN<#828#> is the ability to multiplex and demultiplex signals to and from such network elements. This operation is a necessity, since the incoming or outgoing connection bitrate from the source entity might be either too low or too high for the receiving entity.

A typical scenario where this operation proves vital is when a mobile subscriber makes a call to a subscriber on a<#829#>PSTN<#829#> . The connection bit rate needs to be changed at the <#830#>MSC<#830#> from a wireless connection bitrate to a bitrate suitable for transmission over a<#831#>PSTN<#831#> . In chapter~#chpt:fap#832> section~#sec:Interference#833> more information regarding bit rates is presented.

Network databases

The <#835#>HLR<#835#>, <#836#>AUC<#836#> and <#837#>EIR<#837#> are the three `back-end' databases which store and provide information for the rest of the <#838#>gsm<#838#> network. In this subsection each one of the databases that form part of the `back-end' is discussed briefly and a description is given of the core functions that each database performs in the network.

Home location register

--- The <#840#>HLR<#840#> is a database that permanently stores information pertaining to a given set of subscribers. It needs to store a wide range of subscriber parameters because it is the reference source for anything <#841#>gsm<#841#> subscriber related in the network[#GSMSysEngin##1###].

Subscriber parameters that are stored in the database include billing information, routing information, identification numbers, authentication parameters and subscribed services[#GSMSysEngin##1###]. The following information is also stored, but the information is of a temporary nature and can change at any time: The current <#844#>VLR<#844#>and <#845#>MSC<#845#> the subscriber is registered with and whether the subscriber is roaming [#GSMSysEngin##1###].

Authentication centre

--- The AUC is the entity in the <#848#>gsm<#848#> network that performs security functions and thus stores information that enables it to provide secure over-the-air communication[#GSM92##1###,#GSMSysEngin##1###]. The information that is stored contains authentication information as well as keys that are used in ciphering information[#GSM92##1###,#GSMSysEngin##1###].

During an authentication procedure no ciphering key is ever transmitted over the air; instead a challenge is issued to the mobile that needs to be authenticated. This challenge requires the mobile station to provide the correct <#851#>SRES<#851#> with regard to the random number generated by the <#852#>AUC<#852#>[#GSM92##1###,#GSMSysEngin##1###]. The random number and ciphering keys that are used change with each call that is made; thus an attacker would gain nothing by intercepting a key, since it will change with the next call [#GSMSysEngin##1###].

Each mobile that is registered in the <#855#>HLR<#855#> database needs to be authenticated and each call that is instantiated needs to retrieve keys from the AUC to establish a secure communication link[#GSM92##1###,#GSMSysEngin##1###]. The AUC is sometimes included with <#857#>HLR<#857#> to allow for fast communication between the two databases [#GSMSysEngin##1###].

Equipment identity register

--- The <#860#>EIR<#860#> is a database that stores the <#861#>IMEI<#861#> numbers of all registered mobile equipment that has accessed the network[#GSMSysEngin##1###]. Only information about the mobile equipment is stored, nothing about the subscriber or call is stored in the databas[#GSMSysEngin##1###].

Typically there is only one <#864#>EIR<#864#> database per network and interfaces to the <#865#>HLR<#865#> databases contained in the network[#GSMSysEngin##1###]. The <#867#>IMEI<#867#> are grouped into three categories: white list, black list and the gray list[#GSMSysEngin##1###] The white list contains only the <#872#>IMEI<#872#> numbers of valid <#873#>MS<#873#>; the black list stores the <#874#>IMEI<#874#> numbers of equipment that has been reported stolen and the gray list stores the <#875#>IMEI<#875#> numbers of equipment that has some fault (faulty software, wrong make of equipment)[#GSMSysEngin##1###].

GSM Network Management Entities

In a <#878#>gsm<#878#> network most of the elements that form part of and make the network function are often distributed in a wide geographical area to provide the best network coverage for the customer[#GSMSysEngin##1###].

For a network to function properly and efficiently network engineers need to be kept up to date on the state of the network and be alerted if any problems occur[#GSMSysEngin##1###]. For this purpose there are two systems in the <#882#>gsm<#882#> network architecture that allow for this functionality required by network engineers[#GSMSysEngin##1###].

One system is called the <#884#>OMC<#884#> which is responsible for centralised regional and local operational and maintenance activities[#GSMSysEngin##1###]. The other system is called the <#886#>NMC<#886#> and unlike the <#887#>OMC<#887#> it provides global and centralised management for operations and maintenance of the network supported by the OMCs [#GSMSysEngin##1###].

In the following paragraphs more in depth discussions on the critical functions the <#889#>OMC<#889#> and <#890#>NMC<#890#> perform is presented.

Operational and Management Centre

--- The <#892#>OMC<#892#> is capable of communicating with <#893#>gsm<#893#> network components using two protocols, namely SS7 and X.25[#GSMSysEngin##1###]. The SS7 protocol is usually used when the <#895#>OMC<#895#> is communicating within the <#896#>gsm<#896#> network over short and medium distances[#GSMSysEngin##1###]. The X.25 protocol is used for large external data transfers[#GSMSysEngin##1###]. All communication where the <#899#>OMC<#899#> is involved occurs over fixed line networks and/or leased lines. The <#900#>OMC<#900#> is usually used for day-to-day operation of a network [#GSMSysEngin##1###].

The <#902#>OMC<#902#> has support for alarm handling[#GSMSysEngin##1###]. An alarm in a <#904#>gsm<#904#> network goes off whenever a predefined expected condition does occur. Engineers are able to define the severity of an alarm, which defines who or what is further alerted and if the alarm needs to be escalated to a higher level [#GSMSysEngin##1###].

The <#906#>OMC<#906#> is also capable of fault management in the <#907#>gsm<#907#> network[#GSMSysEngin##1###]. It is able to activate, deactivate, remove and restore a service manually or automatically on network devices[#GSM92##1###]. Various tests can be run and diagnostic information can be retrieved on the network devices to detect any current or future defects [#GSMSysEngin##1###].

Network Management Centre

--- The <#912#>NMC<#912#> is similar to the <#913#>OMC<#913#> but it is not restricted to only regional <#914#>gsm<#914#> network components as it is in charge of all the <#915#>gsm<#915#> network components in the network[#GSMSysEngin##1###]. The <#917#>NMC<#917#> provides traffic management for the global network and also monitors high priority alarms such as overloaded or failed <#918#>gsm<#918#> network components[#GSMSysEngin##1###]. It is usually used in long-term planning of a network, but it has the capability to perform certain <#920#>OMC<#920#> functions when a <#921#>OMC<#921#> is not staffed.


GSM Interfaces

In the <#924#>gsm<#924#> network all the network components communicate with each other through predefined interfaces. In this section an overview is given of these interfaces between the components.
<#983#>Um interface<#983#> --- This interface is the link between an <#927#>MS<#927#> device and a <#928#>BTS<#928#> and is also referred to as the Air interface since communication occurs wirelessly. The primary protocol used on this interface is the <#930#>LAPDm<#930#>, which is an extension of the <#931#>ISDN<#931#> LAPD protocol to accommodate the mobile nature of <#932#>MS<#932#> devices as well as for the shorter <#933#>TDMA<#933#> frames which are used in <#934#>gsm<#934#> networks[#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###,#GSMSecurInTeleNetwork##1###].
<#984#>Abis interface<#984#> --- Between the <#937#>BTS<#937#> and <#938#>BSC<#938#> the interface used for communication is known as the Abis interface. The only messages that the <#939#>BTS<#939#> is interested in are those that have to do with management of radio resources[#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###,#GSMSecurInTeleNetwork##1###]. All other messages are left alone and merely pass through the <#941#>BTS<#941#> to the <#942#>BSC<#942#> transparently.
<#985#>A interface<#985#> --- The interface between the <#944#>BSC<#944#> and <#945#>MSC<#945#> is known as the A interface. This interface is used for the transfer of information, which is used by the <#946#>MSC<#946#> to manage BSSs, control connections and manage the mobility of <#947#>MS<#947#> in its area of service[#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###,#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].
<#986#>Other Interfaces<#986#> --- The <#950#>MSC<#950#> has interfaces going from itself to databases and other external networks. Each interface connects to a specific database, <#951#>MSC<#951#> or network and is therefore very specific as to what function it performs[#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###,#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. An interface going from one <#953#>MSC<#953#> to another will convey information regarding handling the administration of handover of an <#954#>MS<#954#> device leaving one administrative area to another MSC's administrative area. The administrative area of an Msc is the section of <#955#>BSS<#955#> components the Msc is responsible for. The handover procedure is a very delicate process which is described in section~#sec:handover#956>.

In this section a brief overview was given of the most critical interfaces used by all the network components of the <#958#>gsm<#958#> network involved. In the next section the difference between a logical channel and a frequency is described. Additionally an outline and overview of all the logical channels defined in the <#959#>gsm<#959#> is presented.


GSM Channels

<#962#>gsm<#962#> defines a series of logical channels, which are used for communication over these interfaces. A distinction needs to be made between channels and frequencies. As discussed earlier, a network is licensed in a certain section of the wireless spectrum for use for commercial communication. This piece of spectrum is referred to as bandwidth and is measured in Hz, therefore #tex2html_wrap_inline14912# mathend000# Hz, where #tex2html_wrap_inline14914# mathend000# denotes the allocated bandwidth[#FundamentalsWirelessCommunication##1###]. This bandwidth W is then divided into N smaller chunks of bandwidth called narrowband chunks. Each N narrowband chunk is a channel and has a width of #tex2html_wrap_inline14916# mathend000# Hz[#FundamentalsWirelessCommunication##1###].

Using <#965#>TDMA<#965#> the <#966#>gsm<#966#> system is able to provide additional transmission capacity by dividing the frequency into eight equal timeslots [#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###]. As can be observed from figure~#fig:GSMChannels#968> each <#969#>TDMA<#969#> frame has a series of consecutive timeslots. Each timeslot can be used for both uplink and downlink transmission. A <#970#>gsm<#970#> channel is a logical channel and refers to a single timeslot within a <#972#>TDMA<#972#> frame [#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###,#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

<#14917#>Figure<#14917#> 2.4: <#14918#>TDMA frame and logical channels [#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###]<#14918#>
#figure974#
The <#1131#>gsm<#1131#> system is therefore able to use the same physical frequency in eight different timeslots without interference as these logical channels are used at different times. Therefore using <#1133#>TDMA<#1133#> the available channels that can be used for communication in <#1134#>gsm<#1134#> are increased eightfold [#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###].

Frequencies are assigned to the uplink and downlink portion of the connection with a duplex separation of 45 Mhz in the frequency band to avoid interference between uplink and downlink. There are two types of channels, traffic channels and control channels. <#1136#>TCH<#1136#> primary purpose is to enable communication of user speech and data and therefore carry no control information [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

A <#1138#>TCH<#1138#> is assigned to an <#1139#>MS<#1139#> device when the device indicates that it needs to communicate with another device either with speech or data. When an <#1140#>MS<#1140#> has finished with the <#1141#>TCH<#1141#> the allocated <#1142#>TCH<#1142#> is reclaimed for use by other <#1143#>MS<#1143#> devices on the network. This request by the <#1144#>MS<#1144#> device occurs using the control channels [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

Control channels are much more actively used in a <#1146#>gsm<#1146#> network since they are the primary means by which control and management of the network occurs [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. These channels are used even when the <#1148#>MS<#1148#> has no active connection and is in idle mode. This constant activity on the control channels is to keep the network updated with information such as the position of the <#1149#>MS<#1149#> (location updating) and signal strength [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###,#GSMSysEngin##1###,#Eisenblatter##1###].

The control channels are divided into three main channel groups namely <#1151#>BCH<#1151#>, <#1152#>CCCH<#1152#> and <#1153#>DCCH<#1153#> [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. Each of these channel groups contains other channels that aid in the control and management of the network. Each group along with the associated channels will now be briefly discussed.

The first group, <#1155#>BCH<#1155#>, consists of three channels:

<#1429#><#1157#>BCCH<#1157#><#1429#> --- This channel is broadcast using the very first frequency assigned to a cell. Using this frequency, the channel broadcasts information regarding the network. This information includes radio channel configuration of the current and neighbouring cells, synchronisation information, registration identifiers and most importantly the format of the <#1158#>CCCH<#1158#> used by the local <#1159#>BTS<#1159#> [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].
<#1430#><#1161#>FCCH<#1161#><#1430#> --- Synchronisation information is broadcast to the <#1162#>MS<#1162#>to enable them to perform frequency correction on the transmission. Typical synchronisation information on this channel, for instance, is the exact frequency the local <#1163#>BTS<#1163#> is using for transmission to enable the <#1164#>MS<#1164#>to attune themselves to the same frequency [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].
<#1431#><#1166#>SCH<#1166#><#1431#> --- On this channel, identifying information regarding the <#1167#>BTS<#1167#> is transmitted. Also on this channel information regarding synchronisation of frames is sent which aids an <#1168#>MS<#1168#> to, for example, structure the time frames of <#1169#>TDMA<#1169#> frames.

The <#1171#>FCCH<#1171#> and <#1172#>SCH<#1172#> are always broadcast with the <#1173#>BCCH<#1173#> since these channels are needed for the operation of the radio subsystem [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. The <#1175#>CCCH<#1175#> is a point-to-point signalling channel that is used to localise an <#1176#>MS<#1176#> through the use of paging[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

When a <#1178#>MS<#1178#> is paged in a <#1179#>gsm<#1179#> network, a <#1180#>CCCH<#1180#> message is sent to all the cells in the surrounding area of the last known cell the <#1181#>MS<#1181#> was recorded to be at[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. The messsage is sent to surrounding cells in case the <#1183#>MS<#1183#> changed location since it was last communication with the network[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. The <#1185#>MS<#1185#> recevies the paging through the <#1186#>CCCH<#1186#> and responds if it is intened for the particular MS[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. The channel is also used to assign dedicated channels [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. The <#1189#>CCCH<#1189#> is made up of the following channels:

<#1432#><#1191#>RACH<#1191#><#1432#> --- The <#1192#>RACH<#1192#> forms the uplink portion of the <#1193#>CCCH<#1193#> and is randomly accessed by the <#1194#>MS<#1194#>to request a dedicated channel for a single signalling transaction [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].
<#1433#><#1196#>AGCH<#1196#><#1433#> --- The <#1197#>AGCH<#1197#> froms the downlink part of the <#1198#>CCCH<#1198#>. It is used by the radio subsystem to assign a <#1199#>SDCCH<#1199#> or <#1200#>TCH<#1200#> to an <#1201#>MS<#1201#> [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].
<#1434#><#1203#>PCH<#1203#><#1434#> --- The <#1204#>PCH<#1204#> also forms part of the downlink portion of the <#1205#>CCCH<#1205#>. This channel is used by the radio subsystem to page specific <#1206#>MS<#1206#>, which aids in the process of locating an <#1207#>MS<#1207#> [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].
<#1435#><#1209#>NCH<#1209#><#1435#> --- This channel is used to inform an <#1210#>MS<#1210#> of any incoming group calls or calls that are being broadcast [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

The last group of signalling channels is referred to as <#1213#>D/ACCH<#1213#>. This group of channels has the characteristic that they are a group of bidirectional point to point channels [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

<#1436#>Stand-alone dedicated control channel (<#1216#>SDCCH<#1216#>)<#1436#> --- This channel is used for communication between the <#1217#>BSS<#1217#> and <#1218#>MS<#1218#> even when there is no active connection[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. Hence the `stand-alone' since it means that there need not be a <#1220#>TCH<#1220#> assigned for communication to occur between the <#1221#>BSS<#1221#> and <#1222#>MS<#1222#> [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].
<#1437#><#1224#>SACCH<#1224#><#1437#> --- When a <#1225#>TCH<#1225#> or <#1226#>SDCCH<#1226#> is assigned, an accompanying <#1227#>SACCH<#1227#> is also assigned. The <#1228#>SACCH<#1228#> is used to transmit information for optimal radio operation, which can include information on power control of the radio transmitter and synchronisation information[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. Packets must be continuously sent over the <#1230#>SACCH<#1230#> as it is used as proof that there is still a physical radio connection [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. When the <#1232#>MS<#1232#> has finished using the <#1233#>SACCH<#1233#> channel, it transmits a report regard the current results of the radio signal level, which is continuously measured [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].
<#1438#><#1235#>FACCH<#1235#><#1438#> --- When more bandwidth is required for signalling purposes, the signal of the <#1236#>TCH<#1236#> is modified using dynamic pre-emptive multiplexing. The additional bandwidth comes at the expense of the user data transport[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. When a channel is created in this manner it is called a <#1238#>FACCH<#1238#> [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

Finally, one last channel is defined namely the cell <#1241#>CBCH<#1241#>, which shares the same physical channel that the <#1242#>SDCCH<#1242#> uses. On this channel messages of the short message service cell broadcast are broadcast[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

In this section an overview was given of how logical channels are used for communication between an <#1245#>MS<#1245#> and <#1246#>BSS<#1246#>/<#1247#>MSC<#1247#>. These three groups of channels collectively enable the <#1248#>gsm<#1248#> network to facilitate wireless communication, a very important function for a telecommunication network. The following section dealts with how the network is able to keep a connection to an <#1249#>MS<#1249#> alive and allow the <#1250#>MS<#1250#> to make calls while the device is moving around geographically within the network.


Handover

The handover process in a <#1253#>gsm<#1253#> network is initiated when an <#1254#>MS<#1254#> with an active call moves outside the coverage area of a cell, <#1255#>BSS<#1255#> or <#1256#>MSC<#1256#> [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###,#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###,#Eisenblatter##1###]. A handover might also be initiated because of measurements indicating bad channel quality[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

Various information needs to be migrated across and shared between the components handling the calls to ensure a smooth handover. Not only the <#1259#>gsm<#1259#> architecture components that need to continuously share information but also the <#1260#>MS<#1260#>. The <#1261#>MS<#1261#> is required to continuously observe and measure signal strength of up to 6 neighbouring cells. The <#1262#>MS<#1262#> does this by monitoring the <#1263#>BCCH<#1263#>[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###,#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###]. This information is of course relayed to the <#1265#>MSC<#1265#> and <#1266#>BTS<#1266#>. The information plays a critical role in the decision process as to which entity will be the best in taking over the administration of the active call[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###,#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###].

An <#1268#>MS<#1268#> can in some cases receive the same <#1269#>BCCH<#1269#> from different cells, which are most likely neighbours [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. This problem of duplicate <#1271#>BCCH<#1271#> from different cells can be attributed to the frequency reuse in the cellular network as well as to the smaller sector cells forming clusters and therefore overlapping coverage area [#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. As discussed in section~#def:cellsector#1273>, cells are divided into sectors, which lessen the problem of co-channel interference. This division into sectors can cause clustering of cells and overlapping of coverage area.

This creates a problem for the <#1274#>MS<#1274#> since it needs to distinguish between the two cell measurements as it does not know which measurement belongs to which cell[#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. To distinguish between different cells, the <#1276#>MS<#1276#> also tries to determine the identity of each cell it is monitoring. Only cells whose identity can be determined reliably are included in the report sent to the BTS[#Eisenblatter##1###,#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###,#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###]2.4. Using this report the handover algorithm is able to decide how to handle the handover, and which cell needs to take over the call[#Eisenblatter##1###,#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###,#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###].

Once the cell has been selected, the actual handover process starts. Which has to take into account that the frequency allocated to the incoming call from the other cell does not interfere with other present active calls in the cell receiving the handover[#Eisenblatter##1###,#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###,#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###]. A frequency interfering with calls currently active in the cell can cause users to hear other conversations from the interfering call, or experience their call being ``dropped'' i.e. disconnected[#Eisenblatter##1###].

There are three core handover procedures when a handover needs to occur in a <#1282#>gsm<#1282#> network: intra-BSC, inter-BSC and inter-MSC, each of which involves different <#1286#>gsm<#1286#> network components [#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###].

<#1425#>Intra-BSC<#1425#> --- Also known as intercell handover, this handover is concerned with the transfer of an active call/connection from an <#1291#>MS<#1291#> to another cell which is controlled by the same <#1292#>BSC<#1292#> as the current cell[#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###,#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. The current <#1294#>BTS<#1294#> that manages the active call of the <#1295#>MS<#1295#> constructs a report containing measurement information from the MS, as well as measurements the <#1296#>BTS<#1296#> has taken on signal strength and error bit ratio of the current connection[#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###,#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. The error bit ratio is defined as how often part of the data being sent over a connection needs to be retransmitted because an error occured[#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###,#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. An error can be caused by interference, faulty hardware or the signal strenght is fading[#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###,#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

The constructed report is forwarded to the managing <#1300#>BSC<#1300#>. The report is analysed to determine the necessity of handing over the active call to another <#1301#>BTS<#1301#>. If a handover is deemed necessary, the <#1302#>BSC<#1302#> starts by initialising the <#1303#>BTS<#1303#> to prepare it to handle the new connection[#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###,#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

The <#1305#>BSC<#1305#> then notifies the <#1306#>MS<#1306#> through the old <#1307#>BTS<#1307#> of the new <#1308#>BTS<#1308#> identity, and properties of the new connection such as <#1309#>TCH<#1309#> frequency, power output etc. After receiving the information about the new connection, the <#1310#>MS<#1310#> makes the necessary adjustments for it to continue operating and handling the call on the new connection[#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###,#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

Once all the adjustments have been made the <#1312#>MS<#1312#> sends a confirmation to the <#1313#>BSC<#1313#> of the successful handover through the new <#1314#>BTS<#1314#>. The <#1315#>BSC<#1315#> instructs the old <#1316#>BTS<#1316#> that it must relinquish the use of the <#1317#>TCH<#1317#> and its associated <#1318#>SDCCH<#1318#> used by the old <#1319#>MS<#1319#> call. The <#1320#>BSC<#1320#> notifies the <#1321#>MSC<#1321#> of the handover as it is used in network operation reports[#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###,#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

<#1426#>Inter-BSC<#1426#> --- In an inter-<#1324#>BSC<#1324#> handover a call of an <#1325#>MS<#1325#> that is being managed by a <#1326#>BTS<#1326#> is transferred to another <#1327#>BTS<#1327#> which has a different controlling <#1329#>BSC<#1329#>. Thus, the call is essentially moved between two <#1330#>BSC<#1330#>, and it is up to the new control <#1331#>BSC<#1331#> to select a suitable <#1332#>BTS<#1332#> that will actually handle the call of the <#1333#>MS<#1333#> being handed over[#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###,#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

This handover occurs because the <#1335#>MS<#1335#> is moving or is about to move into a cell that is not controlled by the current <#1336#>BSC<#1336#>. The current <#1337#>BSC<#1337#> detects this and therefore takes the necessary precautions to ensure that the new <#1338#>BSC<#1338#> is able to make suitable provision to assume control of the call within one of its BTSs[#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###,#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

The <#1340#>BSC<#1340#> informs the managing <#1341#>MSC<#1341#> that a handover to another <#1342#>BSC<#1342#> must occur. The request sent to the <#1343#>MSC<#1343#> contains the identity of the cell managed by a different BSC. The <#1344#>MSC<#1344#> determines the managing <#1345#>BSC<#1345#> of the cell in question and notifies it that it must select and prepare the cell for handover. The new <#1346#>BSC<#1346#> informs the cell to create a new connection, which will be used by the <#1347#>MS<#1347#> once the handover is complete[#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###,#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

The new <#1349#>BSC<#1349#> informs the <#1350#>MSC<#1350#> of the new connection details which the cell will use to handle the handover and maintain the active connection of the <#1351#>MS<#1351#>[#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###]. The <#1353#>MSC<#1353#> forwards the connection information to the old <#1354#>BSC<#1354#>, which forwards it to the <#1355#>MS<#1355#>[#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###]. The <#1357#>MS<#1357#> makes the necessary adjustments for it and then moves on to the new connection. The <#1358#>MS<#1358#> informs the new <#1359#>BSC<#1359#> that the handover has been completed successfully[#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###]. The new <#1361#>BSC<#1361#> informs the <#1362#>MSC<#1362#>, which then instructs the old <#1363#>BSC<#1363#> to relinquish the old connection used by the <#1364#>MS<#1364#>[#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###,#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

<#1427#>Inter-MSC<#1427#> --- With an inter-<#1367#>MSC<#1367#> handover, control and management of an active call on an <#1368#>MS<#1368#> must be transferred to another <#1369#>BTS<#1369#>, which resides in a different area that is managed by a different MSC[#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###]. The handover process follows the same basic formula as the intra-<#1371#>BSC<#1371#> and inter-<#1372#>BSC<#1372#> handovers once the handover request is made[#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###,#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###]. The <#1374#>BSC<#1374#> managing the <#1375#>BTS<#1375#> to which the <#1376#>MS<#1376#> is going to be handed over is to be determined by the MSC[#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###]. The <#1378#>BSC<#1378#> is notified by the new managing <#1379#>MSC<#1379#> that certain <#1380#>BTS<#1380#> must bring a new connection online for the incoming <#1381#>MS<#1381#>; the network components upstream2.5 are notified[#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###,#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

The new <#1386#>MSC<#1386#> informs the old <#1387#>MSC<#1387#> of the new connection details. The old <#1388#>MSC<#1388#> transfers the connection information to the <#1389#>MS<#1389#> that is going to be a handed over to another <#1390#>BTS<#1390#>[#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###]. The <#1392#>MS<#1392#> makes the corresponding adjustments and then starts operating on the new connection. The <#1393#>MS<#1393#> informs the <#1394#>BSC<#1394#> of the successful handover[#wirelesstelcoMullet##1###,#GSMArchitectureProtocolsServices##1###].

The <#1396#>BSC<#1396#> in turn informs the new <#1397#>MSC<#1397#>, which in turn informs the old <#1398#>MSC<#1398#> that the handover was successful. The <#1399#>MSC<#1399#> then instructs the <#1400#>BSC<#1400#> to ensure that the old connection resources are relinquished by the <#1401#>BTS<#1401#>.

In this section a discussion was presented on the handover process in <#1403#>gsm<#1403#> network. The effect this has on channel selected as well as the different handover procedures was highlighted.

Summary

In this chapter a broad discussion was given of modern cellular technology, specifically <#1405#>gsm<#1405#> cellular network technology. A brief history on how <#1406#>gsm<#1406#> was developed to be the most widely used cellular technology in use today was provided. The <#1407#>gsm<#1407#> architecture components followed. In the <#1408#>gsm<#1408#> architecture all the network components present in a modern <#1409#>gsm<#1409#> network were identified.

Following the discussion of the <#1410#>gsm<#1410#> network components, a broad overview was given of the communication interfaces used between the <#1411#>gsm<#1411#> network components to communicate with each other. This was followed by a definition of the <#1412#>gsm<#1412#> channels which are used on the interfaces to communicate information.

The chapter concluded with the handover process which is used to allow an <#1413#>MS<#1413#> device to move freely geographically within the network.


The Frequency Assignment Problem

Introduction

The <#1502#>FAP<#1502#>3.1 is a generalisation of the graph-colouring problem and is consequently an NP-Complete problem[#FAPRAMColouring##1###]. The <#1507#>FAP<#1507#> is an NP-Complete problem due to fact that only a finite number of frequencies can be assigned to <#1508#>TRX<#1508#>, where the number of transceivers to be assigned frequencies greatly outweighs the number of available frequencies[#FAPRAMColouring##1###]. A more thorough definition of what it means for a problem to be NP-Complete is given in section~#sec:NPComplete#1510>.

In wireless communication a huge concern is interference which occurs when frequencies used for communication are close to each other in the frequency spectrum[#Karen2004##1###]. Interference and its effects is discussed in detail in section~#sec:Interference#1512>. Essentially for the <#1513#>FAP<#1513#> the primary concern is to develop an approximate plan on assigning frequencies in such a way that interference is kept to a minimum.

Using exact algorithms to find a solution is not practical since the time to find a solution is polynomial. Generally metaheuristic algorithms are used to find optimal solutions to NP-Complete problems[#ACOvsEA##1###]. In the chapter~#chpt:heuristic#1515>, a discussion is presented on algorithms that are used to find solutions to NP-Complete problems.

As discussed in chapter~#chpt:celltech#1516> network operators are licensed a range of frequencies from the available spectrum. A licensed piece of spectrum contains a series of consecutive frequencies as well as gaps. Gap frequencies are barred from being used by any device within the network as they may have already been allocated to another operator for use [#FAPInCell##1###]. By barring frequencies, a scenario is avoided where the different networks' equipment interferes with their respective operations[#FAPInCell##1###].

Due to the whole spectrum not being available to network operators and only a subset being available for commercial communication as per the frequencies allocated to them, networks opt to reuse their frequencies[#FAPInCell##1###]. The networks do this to maximise the use of their allocated frequencies and to minimise their licensing fees, since if the network needs more frequencies, they need to be licensed[#FAPRAMColouring##1###].

It is not always possible to simply allocate more frequencies to a network even if the network pays the associated fees. The whole commercial spectrum may already have been licensed to various entities. Hence, licensed frequencies are a very valuable and scarce commodity [#FAPRAMColouring##1###,#FAPInCell##1###].

The chapter is organised as follows. The first section the follows provides a discussion on NP-Complete problems. In section~#sec:chm#1522> constraint handling mechanisms are presented. Section~#sec:FreqAssignmentTypes#1523> presents an overview of different methods for allocated frequencies. Interference is discussed in depth in section~#sec:Interference#1524>. After the interference discussion a section is presented on the different types of frequencies assignment problems in section~#sec:FAPVariants#1525>. A mathematical formulation of the fixed spectrum frequency assignment problem is presented in section~#sec:FAPMathDef#1526>. Section~#sec:FAPBenchmarks#1527> provides an overview of the different benchmarks used to evaluate frequency assignment problems. After the discussion on the different benchmarks that are available in the domain a section on the where the FAP is encountered in the industrial domain is presented.

This chapter concluded with a section that summarises the contents presented. In the next section an overview of what it means when a problem is NP-Complete is given.


NP-Complete

The term NP stems from the field known as complexity analysis. Algorithms are typically measured for their worst running time using #tex2html_wrap_inline14932# mathend000# notation. The field of complexity analysis is more interested in measuring complexity of a problem than the running time of an algorithm[#AIModernApproach##1###].

The field of complexity analysis makes a keen distinction between problems that can be solved by algorithms in polynomial time and problems that cannot be solved in polynomial time using any available algorithm[#AIModernApproach##1###]. An algorithm is said to be of polynomial time if the number of steps it needs to perform to solve the problem is given by #tex2html_wrap_inline14934# mathend000# where #tex2html_wrap_inline14936# mathend000# is a nonnegative integer and #tex2html_wrap_inline14938# mathend000# refers to the complexity of the input[#AIModernApproach##1###].

A distinction needs to be made between finding a possible solution and determining whether a result is a valid solution to an NP problem[#AIModernApproach##1###]. Verifying whether some result is indeed a solution to an NP problem is a quick operation[#AIModernApproach##1###]. Finding a solution through the use of an algorithm is what polynomial time refers to.

Algorithms that are able to solve problems in polynomial time have worst case running times of #math49##tex2html_wrap_inline14940# mathend000# and #tex2html_wrap_inline14942# mathend000#[#AIModernApproach##1###]. These classes of problems are easy to solve using modern algorithms available today. Problems that can be solved with these kind of running times are classified as being in the P range of complexity problems[#AIModernApproach##1###].

Another range of problems consists mostly of problems that cannot be solved in polynomial time[#AIModernApproach##1###]. Hence, if an algorithm were to try each and every possible solution, it would take an arbitrarily long time, which cannot be determined, which is why these problems also have the characteristic of being non-deterministic[#AIModernApproach##1###]. Problems in this range are referred to as being in the NP range of complexity problems[#AIModernApproach##1###].

There is another range of NP problems that are a subset of NP problems, which are referred to as the ``most extreme'' problems within NP. These problems are collectively known as NP-Complete problems and are the most difficult problems to determine feasible solutions for in the NP problem range[#AIModernApproach##1###]. The <#1542#>FAP<#1542#> is one such problem [#AndreasPaper##1###,#FixedFAPPSO##1###].


Constraint Handling mechanisms

Not only is the <#1546#>FAP<#1546#> an NP-Complete problem but it is also a constraint problem. Constraint problem restrict the search space of possible solutions with boundaries. These boundaries are reffered to as constraints. The <#1547#>FAP<#1547#> domain has specific constraints defined for each of the different individual problems. Before the various ways in which frequencies can be allocated is discussed a brief discussion needs to be presented on constraint handling mechanisms.

Constraints define the boundaries of the search space. Solutions found to be violating the constraints are considered to be outside of the search space. Various methods exist how to treat solutions found to be outside of the defined search space. These methods are known to be constraint handling mechanisms and are discussed below.

In research done by Engelbrecht[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###] the following constraint handling mechanisms are defined.

  • <#1626#>Reject infeasible solutions<#1626#> --- Solutions that violate the defined constraints are not even considered and rejected.
  • <#1627#>Penalty function methods<#1627#> --- Modifies the objective function to add a penalty that is enforced if a solution violates constraints. By adding a penalty the infeasible solutions are discouraged.
  • <#1628#>Convert the constrained problem to an unconstrained problem<#1628#> --- By solving the unconstrained problem better solutions can be found although not guaranteed.
  • <#1629#>Preserving feasibility methods<#1629#> --- Candidate solutions are initialised in the search space and satisfy all constraints. These solutions are continuously moved or transformed with specialised operators who ensure that the solutions continue to satisfy all the constraints. The operators ensure that all solutions stay within the bounded search space.
  • <#1630#>Pareto ranking methods<#1630#> --- Solutions are ranked based on the severity of their constraint violations.
  • <#1631#>Repair methods<#1631#> --- Operators are used to transform solutions that violate constraints to solutions that adhere to all the constraints and are thus feasible solutions.

Constraint handling methods need to be used when solving constrained problems like the <#1558#>FAP<#1558#>. Without these methods, the search for solutions is undirected and solutions that cannot be used are presented as most optimal. Reference to these methods will be made where applicable.

In the next section, two methods are discussed that define how frequencies are allocated.


Frequency Allocation Types

In this section the different methods used to allocate frequencies to cells in a cellular network are discussed. Furthermore the method that relates to the specific <#1561#>FAP<#1561#> variant in this dissertation is described.

Within the <#1562#>FAP<#1562#> domain there are different types of <#1563#>FAP<#1563#>, which have emerged over the years as the domain of wireless communications matured and technological requirements changed. These <#1564#>FAP<#1564#> variants are discussed in section~#sec:FAPVariants#1565>.

There are a variety of <#1566#>FAP<#1566#>s in the wireless communication domain but each individual problem can be classified into one of the following two categories based on the way frequencies are assigned to cells:

  • <#1568#>FFA<#1568#> is where frequencies assigned to cells are static; therefore they cannot be changed until a new assignment plan is calculated[#PrinciplesMobileCommunication##1###].
  • <#1570#>DFA<#1570#> is the process of allocating frequencies to cells as required to meet the current traffic demand imposed on them by clients[#PrinciplesMobileCommunication##1###].

Fixed Frequency/Channel Assignment

<#1574#>FFA<#1574#> is the process of permanently assigning frequencies to cells [#PrinciplesMobileCommunication##1###]. The frequencies assigned are fixed and cannot be changed immediately while the network is active, since the frequencies assigned to the cell form part of a delicate frequency plan designed to keep interference on communication links to a minimum[#PrinciplesMobileCommunication##1###].

When the channel used by a particular cell for communication is suddenly changed, the cell might start interfering with neighbouring cells' communication links. This interference is caused because the assigned frequencies of the neighbouring cells are close to each other on the frequency spectrum. Hence, if the cell is sectored (refer to section~#def:cellsector#1577> in chapter two for a discussion on cell sectorisation) it can interfere with a minimum of three and up to a maximum of six neighbouring cells[#PrinciplesMobileCommunication##1###].
