When an <#1579#>FFA<#1579#> plan is created, cells are assigned frequencies based on the estimated traffic that cell will be expected to handle during peak network usage. <#1580#>FFA<#1580#> is ideally suited for macro cellular networks since the nature of the traffic encountered in such networks has the characteristic of being homogeneous, stationary and predictable [#PrinciplesMobileCommunication##1###]. Cellular networks can be classified as being in the macro cellular group of wireless networks.

With <#1582#>FFA<#1582#>, networks are able to permanently allocate a certain subset of frequencies to cells since the nature of the traffic on their network allows them to predict with reasonable certainty the call blocking probability [#PrinciplesMobileCommunication##1###]. A call is blocked on the network when a cell has no available frequencies to use when establishing a communication link [#PrinciplesMobileCommunication##1###].

In situations where the nature of the traffic is neither homogeneous nor stationary, using the <#1585#>FFA<#1585#> allocation scheme is not feasible as its use of available frequencies is grossly inefficient [#PrinciplesMobileCommunication##1###].

A problem that occurs with <#1587#>FFA<#1587#> when all assigned frequencies are in use is that any new call or call handed over will be blocked[#PrinciplesMobileCommunication##1###]. The call will be blocked even if adjacent cells have suitable capacity to handle the call[#PrinciplesMobileCommunication##1###].

Dynamic Frequency/Channel Assignment

<#1591#>DFA<#1591#> is a channel allocation scheme where frequencies assigned to cells are not permanent but rather assigned to cells as the need arises[#PrinciplesMobileCommunication##1###]. Therefore all the frequencies licensed by a particular network are available to each and every cell to establish a communication link as long as the channel does not violate the co-channel reuse constraint [#PrinciplesMobileCommunication##1###].

The co-channel reuse constraint must be adhered to otherwise the amount of interference occurring on the communication link will be too much[#PrinciplesMobileCommunication##1###]. This constraint forms part of the electromagnetic constraints, which are described in section~#sec:Interference#1595>.

The <#1596#>DFA<#1596#> allocation scheme is ideally suited for micro cellular wireless networks. The traffic on these networks have the characteristic of being immensely unpredictable as traffic demand varies constantly[#PrinciplesMobileCommunication##1###,#MobileWirelessCommunications##1###].

As the name indicates, micro cellular wireless networks have much smaller cell sizes than macro cellular networks. Thus a cell in a micro cellular network must handle a lot more handover requests than a cell in a macro cellular network, since an <#1598#>MS<#1598#> with an active connection is much more likely to move out of the coverage area of a micro cell than a macro cell [#PrinciplesMobileCommunication##1###,#WirelessCommunications##1###].

Since a micro cellular network has increased handover requests between cells compared with a macro cellular network, a <#1600#>DFA<#1600#> scheme must rapidly allocate frequencies to requesting cells that must handle the handovers[#PrinciplesMobileCommunication##1###,#WirelessCommunications##1###].

<#1602#>DFA<#1602#> is much more efficient than <#1603#>FFA<#1603#> when the amount of mobile traffic on the network is relatively low[#PrinciplesMobileCommunication##1###,#WirelessCommunications##1###]. On the other hand, when the network is under heavy mobile traffic load, the <#1605#>FFA<#1605#> scheme outperforms the <#1606#>DFA<#1606#> scheme[#WirelessCommunications##1###]. The <#1608#>DFA<#1608#> shceme is outperformed because it allocates frequencies to cells in an inefficient arrangement that might affect the amount of interference encountered on the network[#MobileWirelessCommunications##1###].

Finally <#1610#>DFA<#1610#> inherently requires a great deal more computational power than <#1611#>FFA<#1611#>, since the frequencies need to be selected and allocated with great speed, otherwise the cell requesting a channel will not be able to handle the call and will therefore block the call or drop the call[#PrinciplesMobileCommunication##1###,#WirelessCommunications##1###].

This concludes the discussion on the different allocation schemes used in modern cellular networks. In the next section a description is given of what interference is and why it is important for cellular networks. An overview will also be given of when interference occurs.


Interference

Interference can be defined as any unwanted signal that is received along with a signal of interest[#WirelessDigitalCommunications##1###]. The unwanted signal is said to interfere with the original signal and as a consequence degrades the original signal quality with unwanted information[#WirelessDigitalCommunications##1###].

Interference usually occurs when two or more entities communicate independently on the same channel or on adjacent frequencies[#WirelessCommunications##1###,#WirelessDigitalCommunications##1###]. Other external factors can also contribute to interference on a communication link, such as machines, which inherently produce some sort of electromagnetic distortion, for instance a car's ignition or a big turbine[#WirelessCommunications##1###,#WirelessDigitalCommunications##1###]. end<#1620#>figure<#1620#> Interference that occurs when two signals operate on the same channel can be seen in figure~#fig:sameinterference#1621> and interference that occurs as a consequence of two signals operating on adjacent frequencies can be seen in figure~#fig:adjacentinterference#1622>.

<#14948#>Figure<#14948#> 3.1: <#14949#>Co-channel interference<#14949#>
#figure1623#
<#14951#>Figure<#14951#> 3.2: <#14952#>Adjacent channel interference<#14952#>
#figure1732#

The impact interference will have on an entity that has established a connection and that operates on the same channel or adjacent channel as another entity decreases as the geographic distance between them increases[#InterferenceOrientatedFAP##1###]. Therefore, to minimise the impact interference will have on communication links a separation is defined[#WirelessCommunications##1###]. More specifically, this separation is known as the channel reuse or frequency reuse distance within wireless networks[#WirelessCommunications##1###].

This separation is defined as the minimum number of cells (which must all use different frequencies) between one cell, which has been allocated a channel, and another cell before a cell is allowed to reuse the same channel that another cell has been allocated[#WirelessCommunications##1###,#InterferenceOrientatedFAP##1###].

The separation can be depicted visually as in figure~#fig:seperationgraph#1760> where #math50##tex2html_wrap_inline14955# mathend000# are different frequencies that are assigned to the specific cells. The frequency #tex2html_wrap_inline14957# mathend000# is allowed to be reused since the two cells it is assigned to are separated by three cells (shaded in grey) because the separation for this network was set to three.

<#14958#>Figure<#14958#> 3.3: <#14959#>Frequency Separation<#14959#>
#figure1761#

As discussed earlier, cellular networks are forced to reuse their licensed frequencies multiple times to keep costs to a minimum. Therefore, the design of a cellular network is limited to the defined separation distance between cells as it defines the size of cells that will be in the network[#Eisenblatter##1###,#InterferenceOrientatedFAP##1###]. Smaller cells can lead to a larger separation distance compared with when cells are larger[#WirelessCommunications##1###,#WirelessDigitalCommunications##1###].

Cellular networks use the amount of interference on their networks as a qualitative measure for their <#1802#>QoS<#1802#>[#WirelessCommunications##1###]. A network with high interference would experience a lot of dropped connections/calls, which occurs when the interference is too high to sustain a connection or call for communication; consequently their <#1804#>QoS<#1804#> degrades as interference increases[#WirelessCommunications##1###,#WirelessDigitalCommunications##1###].

Even though interference can cause a call or connection to be lost, i.e. dropped, there are other situations where a call can be dropped[#GSMSysEngin##1###]. A situation where a call can be dropped is when a handover procedure occurs between two cells and one cell receiving the call is at full utilisation of its allocated frequencies[#GSMSysEngin##1###,#WirelessDigitalCommunications##1###]. Another situation can be due to weather conditions. Weather brings forth natural interference or the caller entering a building which drastically reduces cellular reception[#WirelessDigitalCommunications##1###].

In the literature a variety of methods are used to calculate the amount of interference in a network. The <#1809#>SIR<#1809#> ratio is the recommended way of calculating the potential interference at a certain point [#Karen2004##1###].

The <#1811#>SIR<#1811#> equation is actually based on the <#1812#>SINR<#1812#> but since cellular networks are interference limited, the noise is not considered in the interference calculation[#WirelessCommunications##1###]. Noise can be disregarded since the power of interference is much larger than the power of noise[#WirelessDigitalCommunications##1###].

A formulation of the <#1815#>SINR<#1815#> and <#1816#>SIR<#1816#> is as follows:

#tex2html_wrap_indisplay14961# #tex2html_wrap_indisplay14962# (3.1)
#tex2html_wrap_indisplay14963# #tex2html_wrap_indisplay14964# (3.2)

Where #tex2html_wrap_inline14966# mathend000# is the power of the received signal and #tex2html_wrap_inline14968# mathend000# is the power associated with interference from within a cell (intracell interference) and interference from outside a cell (intercell interference)[#WirelessCommunications##1###].

This calculation can be considered a best guess as it models the environment, weather and other factors which may influence the potential interference at a point with a Gaussian distribution with standard deviation for noise represented by the #tex2html_wrap_inline14970# mathend000#[#Karen2004##1###].

Using the <#1825#>SIR<#1825#> formula cellular networks are able to determine the <#1826#>BER<#1826#> users on the network will experience on their connections[#WirelessDigitalCommunications##1###]. The <#1828#>BER<#1828#> is defined as the probability that a received bit on the connection will be incorrect[#MobileWirelessCommunications##1###].

As the <#1830#>BER<#1830#> increases voice quality on the connection decreases since more bits that are used to describe the voice information are incorrectly received[#WirelessDigitalCommunications##1###]. <#1832#>SIR<#1832#> and <#1833#>BER<#1833#> probability are interlinked. As <#1834#>SIR<#1834#> increases, i.e. less interference is encountered on the communication link, the probability that bits will be received incorrectly decreases[#WirelessDigitalCommunications##1###].

Whether precise measurements are taken or the interference is calculated based on the <#1836#>SIR<#1836#> formula, the end result of both methods is that all the calculated or measured values are put into a matrix to produce an interference matrix[#ACOvsEA##1###].

An interference matrix consists of a number of cell pairs (i,j), where i is the cell receiving interference and j the cell whose allocated channel is providing the interference[#Karen2004##1###]. Each cell pair in the matrix has two corresponding values that indicate the level of interference if the electromagnetic constraints are violated [#ACOvsEA##1###,#AndreasPaper##1###].

Primarily interference occurs when the electromagnetic constraints are violated. These constraints are defined as:

<#14971#>Co-channel<#14971#>
--- As discussed earlier, when cell i and cell j operate on the same channel interference will occur [#GSMSysEngin##1###,#PrinciplesMobileCommunication##1###]. When this type of interference occurs it is referred to as co-channel interference.
<#14972#>Adjacent channel<#14972#>
--- When cell i and cell j operate on adjacent frequencies, their allocated frequencies differ by one, i.e. cell i operates on channel f, then if cell j operates on either channel f - 1 or f + 1, then interference will occur[#GSMSysEngin##1###,#InterferenceOrientatedFAP##1###]. This type of interference is referred to as adjacent channel interference.

The electromagnetic constraints defined above are applicable in any wireless network. With regard to mobile telecommunication networks, such as cellular networks, there are additional constraints that are imposed due to technological requirements, availability, location and size of area with unacceptable interference [#Karen2004##1###,#Eisenblatter##1###,#AndreasPaper##1###]. These constraints are defined as the following:

<#14973#>Co-site<#14973#>
--- If cell i and cell j are located at the same site, then their allocated channel ranges must differ by a certain distance in the frequency domain. This distance is known as the reuse distance where cell i and cell j serve different sectors[#FixedFAPPSO##1###,#EgyptFAPPSO##1###]. In the benchmarks which are discussed in section~#sec:FAPBenchmarks#1867> this distance is also referred to as the separation variable.
<#14974#>Co-cell<#14974#>
--- Channels used on the same antennae of a cell must differ by a certain number. This is typically set to three but can be any number greater than zero that the network operator deems necessary to avoid unwanted interference[#Karen2004##1###,#Eisenblatter##1###,#AndreasPaper##1###].
<#14975#>Handover<#14975#>
--- This constraint means that frequencies must differ by a predefined margin, i.e. two or three, when one cell hands over a call to another cell. If this constraint is violated a mobile subscriber will experience a dropped call since the handover between cells fails[#Karen2004##1###,#Eisenblatter##1###,#AndreasPaper##1###].

Within the licences of wireless networks there are two hard constraints which forbid networks from using certain frequencies. Hard constraints means that under no circumstances are these constraints allowed to be violated.

The first set of hard constraint frequencies is known as globally blocked frequencies. Frequencies that are in the set of globally blocked frequencies are usually frequencies that have been licensed to other networks[#Karen2004##1###,#InterferenceOrientatedFAP##1###].

The second set of hard constraint frequencies is known as locally blocked frequencies. These frequencies are not allowed at certain geographic areas but are free for use at any other area[#InterferenceOrientatedFAP##1###]. A typical area where certain frequencies will be forbidden to be used is near a country border[#InterferenceOrientatedFAP##1###]. The locally blocked frequencies are most likely in use by another network resident to the bordered country.

In this section a description was given of what interference is and what the consequences are of too much interference in a network. This section further elaborated on the circumstances in which interference can occur in a wireless network. In the next section an overview is given of the various different sub problems in the <#1877#>FAP<#1877#> domain.


Frequency Assignment Problem types

In this section each of the problem variants for the <#1880#>FAP<#1880#> is discussed, starting with one of the first and oldest problems in the <#1881#>FAP<#1881#> domain. This section will conclude with a discussion on the particular variant of <#1882#>FAP<#1882#> focussed on in this research.

Minimum Order Frequency Assignment Problem

The <#1884#>MO-FAP<#1884#> was the first <#1885#>FAP<#1885#> that emerged in the 1970s. The <#1886#>MO-FAP<#1886#> is concerned with assigning frequencies to transmitters while interference is minimised as well as minimising the number of different frequencies that are used[#Karen2004##1###].

In <#1888#>MO-FAP<#1888#> channel reuse is prioritised and the usage of a channel has a certain cost associated with it. The reason for this is that when the wireless network industry started, operators were billed according to the number of different frequencies they used. In the beginning of commercial cellular networks frequencies were not cheap since they were sold per unit [#MontemanniThesis##1###].

Over the years as the law governing the wireless spectrum changed and new technology as well as standards emerged, <#1890#>MO-FAP<#1890#> lost its relevancy[#Karen2004##1###,#MontemanniThesis##1###]. Companies are no longer billed according to the different frequencies they use, but they purchase licences from a regulatory body[#Karen2004##1###,#MontemanniThesis##1###]. This licence usually stipulates what channel band the network is allowed to use.

In some instances a certain band of frequencies is put up for auction by a regulatory body, on which interested parties can bid to own the specified spectrum[#Karen2004##1###,#MontemanniThesis##1###]. Due to the shift in how frequencies are allocated to networks, neither the regulatory bodies nor the network operators care about the number of different frequencies are used[#Karen2004##1###,#MontemanniThesis##1###].

Minimum Span Frequency Assignment Problem

The <#1896#>MS-FAP<#1896#> is a problem that is very relevant today, especially when network operators want to deploy a new network in a region[#Karen2004##1###]. The <#1898#>MS-FAP<#1898#> is concerned with keeping the interference below a certain level during assignment as well as minimising the span[#MSFAP##1###]. The interference threshold used is specified by the network designer as the minimum allowable interference on the network[#MSFAP##1###].

The span is defined as an interval on the frequency domain. This interval is the difference between the maximum and minimum frequencies used during assignment[#Karen2004##1###,#MSFAP##1###]. With the span value, network operators are able to request certain frequency bands and know their network will be able to operate at suitable interference levels [#Karen2004##1###,#MSFAP##1###].

The <#1903#>MS-FAP<#1903#> and <#1904#>MO-FAP<#1904#> are two very similar problems, the only difference being that <#1905#>MO-FAP<#1905#> focuses on minimising different frequencies and <#1906#>MS-FAP<#1906#> focuses on minimising the interval of frequencies used during assignment [#Karen2004##1###]. The Philadelphia benchmark is usually used to gauge how well the algorithm performs.

Minimum Interference Frequency Assignment Problem

The <#1909#>MI-FAP<#1909#> or <#1910#>FS-FAP<#1910#> is encountered after the network operator has obtained a frequency band from a regulatory body[#Karen2004##1###].

Unlike the previous problems, in <#1912#>MI-FAP<#1912#> any available channel in the allocated band may be used even though it produces interference. The other problems are concerned with the frequencies used, even though they might be violating some constraints that incur a huge amount of interference[#MontemanniThesis##1###,#MultipleBinaryFAP##1###]. The interference value does not play a large role in their respective objective functions[#Eisenblatter##1###,#MultipleBinaryFAP##1###]. In <#1915#>MI-FAP<#1915#> the objective is to minimise the total amount of interference on the network. It is important to note that this amount of interference might not necessarily be zero [#Karen2004##1###,#Eisenblatter##1###].

The <#1917#>MI-FAP<#1917#> is the problem currently most encountered in cellular networks, since there are more operating networks than new networks being designed in the cellular industry today[#Karen2004##1###]. This particular problem forms the focus of this research.

Since <#1919#>MI-FAP<#1919#> is very close to real-world instance problems, authors tend to use real-world instances or benchmarks to test the quality and efficiency of their algorithms [#Eisenblatter##1###,#MontemanniThesis##1###]. The quality and efficiency of the solution in this research is benchmarked against the <#1921#>COST<#1921#> 259 benchmark ,which is discussed in section~#sec:FAPBenchmarks#1922>.

In the following section a formal mathematical definition for the fixed spectrum <#1923#>MI-FAP<#1923#> is set out. The definition is important as it forms the basis for the objective/cost function that the algorithm in this research uses.


FS-FAP Mathematical Formulation

A Mathematical definition of the <#1926#>FAP<#1926#> is given in this section. The mathematical definition is used by the algorithm discussed in this dissertation to evaluate the amount of interference that generated frequency plans exhibit.

The <#1927#>FAP<#1927#> can be represented as a graph colouring problem and is known to be NP-Complete. Before a mathematical definition can be formally given for the <#1928#>FAP<#1928#>, some symbols and their respective definitions need to be introduced.

#tex2html_wrap_indisplay14981# #tex2html_wrap_indisplay14982# (3.3)
#tex2html_wrap_indisplay14983# #tex2html_wrap_indisplay14984# (3.4)
#tex2html_wrap_indisplay14985# #tex2html_wrap_indisplay14986# (3.5)
#tex2html_wrap_indisplay14987# #tex2html_wrap_indisplay14988# (3.6)
#tex2html_wrap_indisplay14989# #tex2html_wrap_indisplay14990# (3.7)
#tex2html_wrap_indisplay14991# #tex2html_wrap_indisplay14992# (3.8)
#tex2html_wrap_indisplay14993# #tex2html_wrap_indisplay14994# (3.9)

Let #tex2html_wrap_inline14996# mathend000# (see equation~#E:setG#1962>) be a weighted undirected graph, where #tex2html_wrap_inline14998# mathend000# (see equation~#E:setV#1963>) is a set of vertices[#MontemanniThesis##1###]. Each #math51##tex2html_wrap_inline15000# mathend000# represents a transmitter in the <#1965#>FAP<#1965#>[#MontemanniThesis##1###].

The variable #tex2html_wrap_inline15002# mathend000# in equation~#E:setE#1967> represents a set of edges[#MontemanniThesis##1###]. An edge consists of two vertices #tex2html_wrap_inline15004# mathend000# and #tex2html_wrap_inline15006# mathend000# that are joined because there is a constraint on the frequencies that can be assigned between the two vertices or transmitter[#MontemanniThesis##1###]s. Each edge has two associated labels #tex2html_wrap_inline15008# mathend000# and #tex2html_wrap_inline15010# mathend000# [#FAPOrientationModel##1###,#TabuMontemanniSmith##1###].

The label #tex2html_wrap_inline15012# mathend000# that is part of the set #tex2html_wrap_inline15014# mathend000# (see equation~#E:setD#1974>) denotes the maximum separation that is required between frequencies assigned to two transmitters #tex2html_wrap_inline15016# mathend000# and #tex2html_wrap_inline15018# mathend000#. #tex2html_wrap_inline15020# mathend000# denotes the frequency assigned to #tex2html_wrap_inline15022# mathend000#. Using equation~#E:interference#1975> the amount of interference that is generated between transmitters #tex2html_wrap_inline15024# mathend000# and #tex2html_wrap_inline15026# mathend000# can be determined[#FAPOrientationModel##1###,#TabuMontemanniSmith##1###].

The other label, #tex2html_wrap_inline15028# mathend000#, forms part of the set #tex2html_wrap_inline15030# mathend000# (see equation~#E:setP#1978>) which is referred to as the interference matrix (discussed in section~#sec:Interference#1979>)[#Eisenblatter##1###]. Each label #tex2html_wrap_inline15032# mathend000# contains two values which represent interference:

  • #math52##tex2html_wrap_inline15034# mathend000# represents the value for co-channel interference [#FAPOrientationModel##1###,#TabuMontemanniSmith##1###].
  • #math53##tex2html_wrap_inline15036# mathend000# represents the value for adjacent channel interference[#FAPOrientationModel##1###,#TabuMontemanniSmith##1###].

Finally the set #tex2html_wrap_inline15038# mathend000# (see equation~#E:setF#1989>) denotes a set of consecutive frequencies for every transmitter in #tex2html_wrap_inline15040# mathend000#[#FAPOrientationModel##1###,#TabuMontemanniSmith##1###].

Formally the <#1991#>FS-FAP<#1991#> can now be defined <#1992#>FS-FAP<#1992#> #math54##tex2html_wrap_inline15042# mathend000# with a required mapping of #math55##tex2html_wrap_inline15044# mathend000#[#TabuMontemanniSmith##1###]. The objective of the <#1994#>FS-FAP<#1994#> is to find an assignment of frequencies to transmitters that minimise the sum of total interference (see equation~#E:costFunction#1995>).

The interference value for a single transmitter is represented by #tex2html_wrap_inline15046# mathend000# which is based on equation~#E:interferenceCases#1996>. In equation~#E:interferenceCases#1997> the co-channel interference value is represented by #math56##tex2html_wrap_inline15048# mathend000# and the adjacent-channel interference value is represented by #math57##tex2html_wrap_inline15050# mathend000#.
