The parent chromosomes are selected with a probability from the mating pool to produce a single chromosome known as the offspring [#FamilyGA##1###,#HumanPassiveGA##1###,#CoactiveFuzzyGA##1###].

The probability is known as the crossover probability and is problem dependent[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. By defining a high crossver probability the good genes that form the current population are retained in the next population[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. With good genes being retained more historical information is passed onto the next generation's population[#FamilyGA##1###].

There are a variety of ways in which genes are interchanged between chromosomes in the crossover operation i.e. fixed point crossover, two point crossover, uniform crossover and gaussian crossover[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. All of these crossovers operate on the premise that a byte representation is used for the chromosomes.

With the byte representation, each chromosome is a byte. Each byte is made up of sequence of bits. As discussed previously each chromosome is made out of genes and therefore each bit is a gene[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###].

Fixed point crossover operates on binary parents whereby a point is selected in one parent and then all other bits are replaced by the other parents' bits [#HumanPassiveGA##1###]. Two point crossover generates two random indices which dictate a certain segment in the one parent to be interchanged with the other parent [#ConstrainedGA##1###]. Where a segment consists of a subset of genes from the chromosome.

The uniform crossover is the most basic of all crossovers since it randomly selects bits from one parent to be replaced by another parent's bits[#ParallelGASA##1###,#GeostatisticalGA##1###]. Finally, the Gaussian crossover also uses the byte representation. The Gaussian crossover interchanges bits between parents based on a Gaussian distribution [#ParallelGASA##1###,#GeostatisticalGA##1###]. Depending on the state of the algorithm, crossover operators can also be interchanged or even paired if the algorithm needs better search performance for large or small solution spaces [#HetergeneousGA##1###,#ParallelGASA##1###].

Caution should be exercised by the crossover operator when selecting chromosomes for reproduction. It is possible for the operator to select the same chromosome twice to be the parent of a single offspring[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. The operator is also at risk of selecting the same chromosome multiple times for reproduction[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. The opertator must therefore incorporate a test to detect unenecessary repeated usage of a chromosome[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. Finally, it is important to note that there exist strategies for both Gaussian crossover and mutation based operators for use with continuous-valued representations[#FundamentalSwarm##1###].

Mutation Operator

The mutation operator is a probabilistic operator and is applied to individials in the offspring population with a probability referred to as the mutation rate[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. The purpose of the mutation operator is to increase the diversity of the genes of a individual chromosomes[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. By introducing new genes into an individual the diversity of the populations characteristics are increased[#CoactiveFuzzyGA##1###,#AcceleratingGA##1###,#ConstrainedGA##1###].

The mutation operator has no previous information on the chromosome it is mutating; thus it is entirely possible that the mutation may modify the chromosome for the worse [#AcceleratingGA##1###]. A worse solution might lead the algorithm out of local optima or lead it down a new path to find the global optima, but this is not always the case [#AdaptiveSAGA##1###,#FamilyGA##1###,#ConstrainedGA##1###]. It is for this reason that it is recommended that the mutation rate be set to a low value to ensure good solutions are not distored too much[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###].

In a survey done by Engelbrecht[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###] another mutation operator is discussed. Instead of mutating a small part of randomly selected chromosomes, this operator generates new offspring to be inserted back into the population. The operator randomly generates a new chromosome and then uses any of the previously discussed crossover operators (see page~#sec:crossover#2978>).

The mutation operator is not always a basic random replacement of genes operation. In research done by Il-kwon Jeong and Ju-jang Lee[#AdaptiveSAGA##1###], a mutation operator is presented that uses the <#2980#>SA<#2980#> algorithm to determine the genes that need to be replaced. The <#2981#>SA<#2981#> mutation operator generates a new chromosome from which genes are used to replace in the chromosome being mutated [#AdaptiveSAGA##1###]. Addition mutation operators are discussed in Engelbrecht[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###].

Initial Population Generation

Initial population generation is the very first activity that the <#2985#>GA<#2985#> performs. Out of this population potential mating candidates are selected based on their fitness, which indicates desirability. The initial population is generated by means of randomisation [#SelfAdaptiveGA##1###]. Since the algorithm searches multiple points simultaneously in the search space, it is desirable that the initial population have a wide diversity with regard to the problem search space[#CombinedBranchBoundGA##1###,#DistributedHierarchicalGA##1###]. By controlling the initial population generation we can control, to a small degree, the amount of exploration the algorithm does initially[#CombinedBranchBoundGA##1###]. Therefore care must be taken in the selection of the particular randomisation scheme that will be used to generate chromosomes for individuals.

In a survey done by Andrea Reese[#RandomNumberGA##1###], two randomisation schemes were defined, namely <#2990#>PRNG<#2990#> and <#2991#>QRNG<#2991#>. <#2992#>PRNG<#2992#> were found to be heavily problem dependent, improving the search efficiency in some instances and in other instances having no considerable impact. QRNGs, on the other hand, were shown to significantly improve the final solution produced by the <#2993#>GA<#2993#> as well as lower the number of generations for the solution to be obtained [#RandomNumberGA##1###].

Algorithm Efficiency

The <#2996#>GA<#2996#> is a powerful, yet simple algorithm and tends to find good solutions given enough time, it does have its disadvantages. One of the major disadvantages occurs when the <#2997#>GA<#2997#> is applied to problems that have very large solution spaces. In these problems, the population size is a very sensitive parameter[#AdaptiveSAGA##1###,#HetergeneousGA##1###,#SelfAdaptiveDataMiningGA##1###,#PatternDetectionGA##1###]. If the population is too small the algorithm will not have enough diversity to search and will tend to converge prematurely.

A large population is preferred in large search spaces in order to get good chromosome diversity among individuals. Hence, the population size must be fine-tuned to achieve optimal performance in large search spaces [#AdaptiveSAGA##1###,#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. Note an increase in population does not guarantee good chromomsome diversity among the population. As discussed in initial populaiton generation it is also dependant on the random generation scheme used as well.

In the next section the pseudo code for the <#3000#>GA<#3000#> algorithm is presented and the flow of the <#3001#>GA<#3001#> algorithm is discussed

Flow of the Algorithm

The core concepts of the genetic algorithm were introduced in the previous section. To better understand the algorithm, a general overview of the algorithm will be presented in this section using algorithm~#alg:GA#3003> as a reference point.
#algorithm3004#
The <#3032#>GA<#3032#> algorithm is a population-based algorithm and therefore needs to initialise its population. Each individual of the population represents a potential solution. Population initialisation occurs in line 1 of algorithm~#alg:GA#3033> on page~#alg:GA#3034>.

The amount of individuals in a population to be generated is predefined and is known as the population size. In algorithm~#alg:GA#3035> on page~#alg:GA#3036> the population size is represented by the subscript #tex2html_wrap_inline15237# mathend000# in #tex2html_wrap_inline15239# mathend000#, where #tex2html_wrap_inline15241# mathend000#.

Before the algorithm can start evolving its population, it first needs to determine each individual in the population's fitness. The fitness of an individual is calculated using a fitness function #math74##tex2html_wrap_inline15243# mathend000#.

After each individual within the initial population has been evaluated, the algorithm is able to start its searching process, which starts at line 3 and ends at line 17 of algorithm~#alg:GA#3039> on page~#alg:GA#3040>.

Since each individual has a fitness value after being evaluated, the selection operator is applied. The selection operator used on line 4 determine which individuals will form part of the parents used to generate offspring for the next population.

Once the selection operator has selected the parent individuals needed for the next population, the algorithm is ready to enter the reproduction phase, which ranges from lines 5 -- 17 of algorithm~#alg:GA#3041> on page~#alg:GA#3042>.

In the reproduction phase the crossover and mutation operators are applied probabilisticly. For the crossover operator a crossover probability is calculated on line 7. Depending if the calculated probability satisfies the crossover probability the crossover operator is applied on line 9. Depending on the crossover used, offspring are generated using one or more individuals as parents. The resulting offspring from the crossover operation is assigned to #tex2html_wrap_inline15245# mathend000#.

On line 11 the value of #tex2html_wrap_inline15247# mathend000# is assigned to the variable #tex2html_wrap_inline15249# mathend000# which represents the offspring. From lines 7 -- 11 it can be observed that a particular individual does not have to take part in a crossover operation to be carried over to the new population. Thus knowledge gained from the current population is persisted based on the crossover propbability.

The second step of the reproduction phase is where the mutation operator is applied. For each of the offspring a mutation probability is calculated. If the calculated probability for a particular offspring are high enough, the algorithm enters the mutation phase. In the mutation phase an individual is selected and the mutation operator is applied. Application of the mutation operator can be seen to occur in lines 13 -- 15 of algorithm~#alg:GA#3046> on page~#alg:GA#3047>.

Regardless of whether the offspring has been mutated or not, the resulting offspring are added to the new population. The reproduction phase continually loops, until the new population equals the size of the initial starting population. Once the amount of individuals in the new population has reached the population size #tex2html_wrap_inline15251# mathend000#, the algorithm moves on to its next iteration.

After the algorithm has completed the reproduction phase the algorithm selects the new population to be used in the next generation. The new population is selected from the current population and the created offspring.

The algorithm continually generates a new population for each generation until a predetermined stopping criterion has been met. Once the criterion has been met, the algorithm selects the individual with the best fitness in the current population as its most optimal solution. The following section discusses literature where the <#3048#>GA<#3048#> has been applied to the <#3049#>FAP<#3049#>.

Genetic Algorithm on the FAP

Continuing the trend of the <#3051#>SA<#3051#> and <#3052#>TS<#3052#> algorithms, the <#3053#>GA<#3053#> has also been applied to a wide variety of problems. These problems include: solving nonconvex nonlinear programming problems[#GANonConvex##1###], data mining [#SelfAdaptiveDataMiningGA##1###] and auto configuring metaheuristic algorithms for complex combinatorial problems [#AutoComplexMeta##1###].

Colombo and Allen[#ProblemDecompMIFAP##1###] have developed a <#3058#>GA<#3058#> to be applied on the <#3059#>FAP<#3059#>. The authors decomposed the <#3060#>FAP<#3060#> into smaller subproblems. On average the solution quality is improved by using the technique but at the expense of more complex and taxing evaluations that have to be performed[#ProblemDecompMIFAP##1###].

In table~#tab:GA#3062> the results obtained by authors on the <#3063#>COST<#3063#> 259 benchmarks are compared. The listed values are scalars representing the total interference generated by the frequency plan.

<#15268#>Table<#15268#> 4.3: <#15269#>GA on <#3070#>COST<#3070#> 259 Benchmark<#15269#>
Problem instance GA Best COST 259
Siemens 1 2.60 2.20
Siemens 2 16.34 14.280
Siemens 3 6.37 5.19
Siemens 4 84.08 81.89


As per the results in table~#tab:GA#3073> the <#3074#>GA<#3074#> produces good results coming close to the best obtained results in the benchmark. By critically reasoning about the <#3075#>GA<#3075#> if it were applied to the <#3076#>FAP<#3076#> in theory, the following disadvantages can be identified:

Diversity

--- The <#3078#>GA<#3078#> continually operates on a set population that is randomly initialised at the beginning of the algorithm. It therefore only has this set of generated genes in the initialised population to therefore evolve successive populations. If one disregards mutation, the <#3079#>GA<#3079#> is a process by which the optimal combinations of the starting genes are found. Thus, the <#3080#>GA<#3080#> purifies the starting population genes in an attempt to find those individual genes, which if combined into a single individual, will produce an optimal individual, i.e. solution. Therefore the <#3081#>GA<#3081#> is very reliant on the quality of the random generator used. The probability of the algorithm finding a particular desirable gene that is exhibited by the starting population is directly related to the random number generator used to initialise the population.

Crossover

--- The crossover operation in the <#3083#>GA<#3083#> is the only means by which successive populations are generated and can therefore be regarded as the primary means by which the algorithm performs its search. As the crossover is defined in the standard algorithm, certain parts of both parents are copied and combined to form a new individual. With regard to the <#3084#>FAP<#3084#>, if each individual represents a frequency plan, the crossover operation would copy certain cells from the two parent plans. This is not desirable, since a single channel within a cell can generate major interference which overshadows the rest of the channels that generate low interference in the cell. Thus, for the <#3085#>GA<#3085#> to generate high quality solutions on the <#3086#>FAP<#3086#>, the algorithm would be better off utilising a crossover operation which works on individual channels assigned rather than cells. Crossover operation is also a memory and computationally expensive operation since individuals need to be constantly created and values need to be copied to these new individuals from the respective parents.

Mutation

--- Mutation is a means by which more diversity is introduced into the chromosomes of the invididuals. As discussed, the mutation operator introduces new genes to existing chromosome which can lead to an excellent solution being distorted and becoming one of the worst solutions. With regard to the <#3088#>FAP<#3088#>, the low probability of mutation is not desirable, as the <#3089#>FAP<#3089#> search space is huge and therefore requires constant diversity to be introduced to accurately explore it. A possible good mutation would be one that is slightly more intelligent than the standard mutation operator, which just randomly modifies a selected individual. An intelligent mutation would be one that takes into account the recent history of the individual as well as the history of the population and, based on the collective knowledge alters or mutates a particular individual. Each chromosome would therefore be required to keep history of changes made to itself, to allow the mutation operator to take it into account. Another means of applying the mutation operator is to set the mutation rate proprtional to an individuals fitness. By modifying the mutation rate in this manner, mutating good individuals can be avoided.

Summary

In this chapter a description was given of metaheuristic algorithms. What it means for an algorithm to be classified as being of a metaheuristic nature was explained as well as the characteristics these algorithms exhibit were identified.

Three metaheuristic algorithms were discussed in this chapter. For every algorithm discussed an explanation was given of how the algorithm works as well as the various characteristics that make the algorithm unique.

For each algorithm, a brief overview of studies using the particular algorithm was given as well as some of the disadvantages or challenges that would be faced when applying the particular algorithm to the <#3092#>FAP<#3092#>.

The first algorithm discussed was the tabu search algorithm and the second was the simulated annealing algorithm. The chapter concluded with the genetic algorithm.

In the table~#tab:summary1#3093>, the algorithms discussed in this chapter and their performance on the <#3094#>COST<#3094#> 259 set of benchmarks have been summarised.

<#15300#>Table<#15300#> 4.4: <#15301#>Summary of algorithm performance on the <#3105#>COST<#3105#> 259 benchmark<#15301#>
Problem instance TS SA GA COST 259
Siemens1 2.7692 23.00 2.60 2.20
Siemens2 14.9360 14.75 16.34 14.280
Siemens3 6.6496 52.55 6.37 5.19
Siemens4 110.9725 80.80 84.08 81.89


In the next chapter the class of algorithm that is relatively new to optimisation research, namely the swarm intelligence class of algorithms, is discussed. #./chpt4.tex# #./chpt5.tex#


Metaheuristic Algorithms: Swarm Intelligence

Introduction

The research field of artificial intelligence stands a lot to gain by the study of the inner workings of nature itself; this is why there is a branch of artificial intelligence that incorporates some of nature's processes, like evolution, which can be seen being applied in practice in the <#3695#>GA<#3695#> (see section #sec:geneticalgorithm#3696>).

There are other approaches in artificial intelligence which also have their routes in nature, for instance animal learning or the study of how dogs learn[#DLearning##1###]. These approaches only look at a “single agent” thought process when agent percepts (senses) are mapped to actions in an agents particular environment [#DLearning##1###].

A percept can be said to be a process that is specifically designed to take data from the surrounding environment, process it and present it as information upon which decisions can be made[#DLearning##1###,#AIModernApproach##1###]. For instance, eyes are percepts, which take visual data presented by the environment[#DLearning##1###,#AIModernApproach##1###]. The visual data is processed by the brain into information to enable decisions to be made on navigating the environment[#DLearning##1###,#AIModernApproach##1###].

The research field of swarm intelligence is an approach more concerned with the underlying processes and behaviour patterns when multiple agents (insects, animals) come together and perform a task as one collective entity[#DLearning##1###,#AIModernApproach##1###]. The study of animals and insects and how they socially interact within their local environments in groups has already contributed to artificial intelligence as a whole in the form of Swarm Intelligence[#ChaoticSwarmIntel##1###,#BeeJobShop##1###]. In the field of Swarm Intelligence, animals and insects are represented by agents which are simple stimulus-response agents and can only perceive changes in their local environments. A group of agents is referred to as a swarm.

Swarm intelligence works on a key aspect observed in nature, the notion of emergent behaviour[#SwarmArt##1###,#FundamentalSwarm##1###]. Emergent behaviour is when an individual in the swarm achieves success due to the it obtaining more knowledge through exploration about its local environment[#SwarmArt##1###,#FundamentalSwarm##1###].

In swarm intelligence, when an agent exhibits emergent behaviour, the outcome of the behaviour needs to be shared with the whole swarm in order for the swarm to adapt and use the newly gained knowledge[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###,#FundamentalSwarm##1###].

In a swarm, the agents communicate with each other about knowledge gained as a consequence of the changes in their local environments[#SwarmArt##1###,#ChaoticSwarmIntel##1###]. Changes can be caused by emergent behaviour by the agent itself, other agents or the environment[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###,#FundamentalSwarm##1###]. Communication among agents facilitates knowledge sharing[#SwarmArt##1###,#FundamentalSwarm##1###].

Social interaction among agents is certainly not the only means of interacting, but with regard to biological inspired systems it is the most prominent[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###].

Using this social interaction between the agents of the swarm can influence their own local environment to move towards more promosing space in the search space [#ChaoticSwarmIntel##1###,#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. Thus each agents of a swarm contributes to the swarm as a whole to locate and produce better solutions [#BeeJobShop##1###]. Due to the social interaction between agents the swarm is also referred to as a social swarm[#ChaoticSwarmIntel##1###,#FundamentalSwarm##1###]

As discussed the behaviour propagates from one agent to another through social interaction, which brings forth information exchange[#SwarmArt##1###]. Social interaction is but one component of self-organisation. Other components that form part of self-organisation are [#SIPowerInNums##1###]:

  • Positive and negative feedback[#SIPowerInNums##1###]
  • Increased fluctuations of random events[#SIPowerInNums##1###]

The means by which agents facilitate indirect communication with each other is known as stigmergy. Stimergy as well as the different forms of stigmergy are discussed in ~#sec:stigmergy#3724>.

Swarm intelligence algorithms are also meta-heuristic algorithms, with the distinction being made that swarm intelligence algorithms use multiple agents as a collective entity of knowledge to search the problem space[#SwarmArt##1###,#ChaoticSwarmIntel##1###,#BeeJobShop##1###,#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###,#FundamentalSwarm##1###].

The initial algorithms developed with regard to swarm intelligence, were based on the coordination and behaviour exhibited by schools of fish and flocks of birds. The newer generation of algorithms include[#SwarmArt##1###,#ChaoticSwarmIntel##1###,#BeeJobShop##1###]:

  • <#3728#>ACO<#3728#>[#SwarmArt##1###].
  • <#3730#>ABC<#3730#>[#BeeJobShop##1###].
  • <#3732#>PSO<#3732#>[#ChaoticSwarmIntel##1###].
  • bacterial foraging optimisation[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###].
  • Firefly optimisation[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###].
  • Fish school optimisation[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###].

Swarm intelligence based algorithms are able to achieve good results since they have simple individuals searching in their own local environments for more optimal solutions[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###,#FundamentalSwarm##1###]. A direct consequence of multiple individuals in a swarm searching is that the algorithms are able to explore multple locations within the defined search space[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###,#FundamentalSwarm##1###]. Traditional single agent based metaheuristic algorithms like <#3740#>TS<#3740#> (section~#sec:tabusearch#3741>) and <#3742#>SA<#3742#> (section~#sec:simulatedannealing#3743>) only have in essence one ``individual'' searching for a solution[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###,#FundamentalSwarm##1###].

With algorithms like <#3745#>TS<#3745#> and <#3746#>SA<#3746#>, the information is not shared since there is only one individual search[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###,#FundamentalSwarm##1###,#SASingleMultiObj##1###,#TSHazardous##1###]. The information is kept to influence future decisions[#AIModernApproach##1###,#TabuMontemanniSmith##1###,#TabuVechicleRoutingWithTimeWindows##1###,#CurveFittingSA##1###,#EcoEquilSA##1###]. Swarm intelligence algorithms also store information for use by the various individuals that make up the swarm to make more informed decisions as the search space is explored[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###,#FundamentalSwarm##1###].

NP-Complete optimisation problems are but one of the fields where swarm intelligence algorithms have been adapted to. Other fields where swarm intelligence has been applied include neural network training[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###], vehicle routing[#ACOSurvey##1###], clustering[#AntSwarmClustering##1###], search engines and electrical power systems[#SAElectricPower##1###]. Swarm intelligence thus seems more suited towards problems with combinatorial complexity[#SIOPDenby##1###].

The chapter is organised as follows. Before the algorithms are discussed an overview of stigmergy is presented in section~#sec:stigmergy#3755>. Starting with section~#sec:ACO#3756> the first swarm intelligence algorithm is discussed namely, Ant Colony Optimisation. Artificial bee colony is discussed in section~#sec:BEE#3757>. Section~#sec:PSO#3758> is a discussion about the Particle Swarm Optimisation algorithm. This chapter concludes with section~#sec:SISummary#3759> that summurises the chapter.


Stigmergy

Stigmergy is defined as the method used by animals and insects to facilitate indirect communication[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###,#AntIntroTrends##1###]. Through the use of stimergy animals or insects are able to socially interact with their own species to convey information to each other[#AntsAndStigmergy##1###,#FundamentalSwarm##1###].

Interaction occurs through signals that the individuals receive which might require them to perform a specific action[#AntsAndStigmergy##1###,#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###,#AntIntroTrends##1###].

Two forms of stigmergy can be observed in nature. One form, sematectonic stigmergy, is a direct and physical form of interaction since it relies on altering the environment[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###].

Examples of this type of stigmergy are nest building and brood sorting by ants[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. Schools of fish also use this type of stigmergy to communicate direction and speed by visually observing their closest partner in the school. Besides using visual information, birds use sound to communicate with and alert each other[#SwarmArt##1###].

The other form, sign-based stigmergy, is an indirect form of interaction, where communication occurs through some sort of signal mechanism[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. Ants use sign-based stigmergy to communicate with each other. More on how ants communicate with this type of stigmergy is discussed in section~#sec:ACOverview#3772>.

Other species that use sign-based stigmergy are bees[#stigmergicoptimization##1###]. When a bee determines that an entity poses a threat to the hive, it might decide to sting the entity. The sting of a bee not only injects a toxin into the entity, but also releases a pheromone[#stigmergicoptimization##1###]. This pheromone alerts nearby other bees from the hive of the presence of an entity that is a potential danger to the hive[#stigmergicoptimization##1###].

The other bees of the hive pick up this pheromone that is released by the initial bee's stinger and attack the entity by also stinging it[#stigmergicoptimization##1###]. As more bees sting the entity, more bee stingers emit the danger pheromone identifying the entity[#stigmergicoptimization##1###]. Hence the pheromone is reinforced and becomes stronger, which persuades more bees into action[#stigmergicoptimization##1###].

Stigmergy is a powerful mechanism that is able to alter the behaviour of a collective entity efficiently, as can be gathered from the above-mentioned examples of stigmergy in nature[#AntsAndStigmergy##1###,#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###,#AntIntroTrends##1###]. Stigmergy is therefore a core concept upon which swarm intelligence algorithms are based as these communication techniques are exploited to aid the algorithm in finding better solutions[#AntsAndStigmergy##1###,#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###,#AntIntroTrends##1###].

In the forthcoming sections three swarm intelligence algorithms are discussed. Each section is divided into four subsections.

First, an overview of the algorithm is given, where basic concepts about the algorithm are introduced as well as a general outline given of the search process the algorithm uses. The second subsection will give an in-depth discussion of some of the core characteristics that make the algorithm unique. The third subsection will provide a step-by-step discussion of the algorithm using pseudocode as a reference.

Finally, for each algorithm studies using the algorithm on the <#3781#>FAP<#3781#> are mentioned and the various considerations that need to be made to apply the algorithm to the <#3782#>FAP<#3782#> are identified.


Ant Colony Optimisation (ACO)


Introduction

ACO is a class of algorithms incorporating different behavioural aspects that ants exhibit when they perform certain activities, i.e. gather food, build nests and construct cemeteries[#AntsAndStigmergy##1###,#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. The first <#3788#>ACO<#3788#> algorithms that were developed were based on the foraging behaviour that was exhibited by ants when finding the most optimal path towards a food source. Deneubourg noticed the foraging behaviour when he performed the bridge experiment [#AntsAndStigmergy##1###,#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###].

The bridge experiment outlined by Deneubourg placed a food source a certain distance away from the nest[#AntsAndStigmergy##1###,#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. Two bridges of equal length were established towards the food resource. The ants initially selected a path randomly with no clear distinction of the more dominant path to take to retrieve food from the food source[#AntsAndStigmergy##1###,#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. After a finite amount of time, one of the paths to the food source became the preferred route for the ants even though both paths were of equal length. The preference of the one bridge by the ants is.

Deneubourg concluded that ants utilise pheromones to communicate to the rest of the foraging ants the shortest path towards a food source.[#FundamentalSwarm##1###] By using pheromones to communicate with other ants it can be concluded that ants use sign-based stigmergy (discussed in section~#sec:stigmergy#3793>) when they retrieve food[#AntIntroTrends##1###,#AntsAndStigmergy##1###,#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. As the ant moves along a particular path, it marks the path with a chemical signal that alerts other ants to the desirability of the path [#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. The chemical signal that ants use to indicate optimal paths is called pheromones[#AntsAndStigmergy##1###,#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###].

The bridge experiment was extended to have two bridges that differ in length. The extended bridge experiment is presented in figure~#fig:antBridgeExperiment#3798> and is known as the shortest path bridge experiement[#FundamentalSwarm##1###]. In the experiment the ants started to prefer the shortest bridge [#FundamentalSwarm##1###]. The conclusion was made that the ants prefered the shorter bridge because ants return to the nest quicker and therefore the path is reinforced with pheromones faster than on the longer path[#FundamentalSwarm##1###].

<#15310#>Figure<#15310#> 5.1: <#15311#>The shortest path bridge experiment [#AntsAndStigmergy##1###]<#15311#>
<#3803#>0pt<#3803#> <#3804#>0.5pt<#3804#> #math75##tex2html_wrap_inline5110# mathend000#

Pheromones used by the ants in <#3809#>ACO<#3809#> go through two phases. The first phase is where pheromones a despoited on a particular path by an ant whether their exists previous pheromones or not. The second phase is where pheromones evapurate. Pheromones are not permenant and deteriorate over time[#FundamentalSwarm##1###]. By letting pheromones evapurate, ants can ``forget'' previous desicions made by die colony[#FundamentalSwarm##1###]. It can be concluded that the more pheromones evapurate the less influence the ants will have on their path selection, therefore promoting exploration[#FundamentalSwarm##1###].

The concept of pheromones and how the <#3813#>ACO<#3813#> proceeds in updating the pheromones is a critical concept of <#3814#>ACO<#3814#>. Hence, an in-depth discussion on pheromones is provided in subsection #sec:ACOcharacter#3815>.

The <#3816#>ACO<#3816#> class of algorithms have a core requirement about the problem they are applied to[#FundamentalSwarm##1###]. The problem must be able to be modelled as a graph. The reason behind this requirement is that each individial ant in the <#3819#>ACO<#3819#> algorithm constructs a path through the graph[#FundamentalSwarm##1###]. The path constructed represents a solution.

A path through a graph is made up of a series of links between nodes[#AIModernApproach##1###,#DataStructuresJava##1###]. A link between two nodes represents a movement from one node to the other[#AIModernApproach##1###,#DataStructuresJava##1###]. Therefore, a path can be considered as the traversal of the interlinked nodes, from a starting node to some final node[#AIModernApproach##1###,#DataStructuresJava##1###]. A path differs from another path by the order in which the nodes are interlinked between a start and end node[#AIModernApproach##1###,#DataStructuresJava##1###].

The <#3825#>ACO<#3825#> class of algorithms has been applied to a wide range of problems that include single machine scheduling[#ACOSingleMachine##1###],weapon target assignment[#WeaponTargetACO##1###], flow shop scheduling[#ACOFlowShop##1###] and image thresholding[#ACOImageThreshold##1###]. Variants of the standard algorithm have been developed, but all of the algorithms still follow the core structure of the <#3830#>ACO<#3830#> algorithm[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###,#FundamentalSwarm##1###]. The first algorithm developed based on the foraging behaviour of ants is known as the <#3832#>SACO<#3832#> and was proposed by Dorigo in 1992 [#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. The algorithm provided the basis for how pheromones are used and updated. The <#3834#>SACO<#3834#> is an algorithmic implementation of the double bridge experiment.

The first algorithm to improve upon the <#3835#>SACO<#3835#> is the <#3836#>AS<#3836#> [#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###,#AntIntroTrends##1###]. The <#3838#>AS<#3838#> included heuristic information into the probability that an ants chooses to move towards a node. The <#3839#>AS<#3839#> also added memory to the <#3840#>AS<#3840#> by using a tabu list and also incorporated pheromone evapuration. The improvements made enabled the <#3841#>AS<#3841#> to better explore the search space and produce better results[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###,#AntIntroTrends##1###].

The <#3843#>AS<#3843#> algorithm has achieved relatively good success in the problems it has been applied to, but it does have some disadvantages[#ImpACOComplex##1###,#ACOSurvey##1###]. One of the primary disadvantages of <#3845#>AS<#3845#> is that it tends to prematuve converge on local optima [#FundamentalSwarm##1###,#ImpACOComplex##1###]. The premature convergence can be attributed to due to the ants exploiting the high concentration of pheromones on good solution paths too quickly[#FundamentalSwarm##1###]. With the ants focusing only on the good solutions less exploration occurs in the search space leading to local optima being produced as the best solution[#FundamentalSwarm##1###].

Subsequently, various algorithms have been developed that improve on the <#3849#>AS<#3849#> algorithm. These improved algorithms include the <#3850#>ACS<#3850#>, <#3851#>MMAS<#3851#>, Ant-Q, fast ant system, AntTabu, <#3852#>AS<#3852#>-rank and i<#3853#>ANTS<#3853#>[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###,#AntIntroTrends##1###]. The discussion in this section is focused on providing an introduction to the general <#3855#>ACO<#3855#> algorithm concepts and not to discuss various improvements made by variants of the core algorithm. In the forth coming sections reference is made to these algorithms and their respective improvements.

The <#3856#>AS<#3856#> algorithm is the base algorithm upon which all other <#3857#>ACO<#3857#> class algorithms are based upon. Therefore in the forth coming sections when a reference is made to the <#3858#>ACO<#3858#> algorithm, it is directed at the base <#3859#>AS<#3859#> algorithm.

In this section the concepts upon which the core of <#3860#>ACO<#3860#> is based upon were briefly introduced. In the next section a discussion is given on each of the concepts.


ACO Characteristics

In this section characteristics that are important and unique to the <#3863#>ACO<#3863#> class of algorithms is discussed. The discussion is focused upon three core characteristics namely pheromone trials, pheromone evaporation, pheromone updates and state transition rules.


Pheromone Trail

The pheromone technique used by ants forms part of the core methodology used by the <#3866#>ACO<#3866#> algorithm [#AntQAP##1###]. As an ant moves it lays down pheromones to mark the path it is walking.

With the use of pheromones ants are able to communicate the best and shortest link between nodes[#AntQAP##1###,#AntsAndStigmergy##1###,#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. The more ants following a preferred link the more pheromones would be deposited on that specific link. This increases the strength of the pheromones [#ImpACOComplex##1###]. The increase in strength of the pheromones on a link would thus let ants more clearly distinguish between links they should and should not take [#ImpACOComplex##1###]. Therefore, a pheromone provides positive feedback to the colony[#AntQAP##1###,#AntsAndStigmergy##1###,#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###].

Initially, all the ants will choose a random link to a node[#AntQAP##1###,#AntsAndStigmergy##1###,#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. After all the ants have completed their paths, each path is evaluated using a cost function defined by the problem domain[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. The amount of pheromone marking the links contained in a path in the standard <#3874#>ACO<#3874#> is related to the cost function[#AntQAP##1###,#AntsAndStigmergy##1###,#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. Therefore, a low cost function value will have a high pheromone dosage and a high cost function value will have a low dosage[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###].

By incorporating the cost of a particular path into the amount of pheromone deposited the colony is able to influcence future decisions[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. In terms of minimisation a path with a low cost will have a high pheromone value making the links of path more likely to be selected by future ants[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###].

In the iterations following the initial one, the ants will at each node decide based on a probability whether it should to a particular neighbouring node. The higher the pheromone intensity is at a neighbouring node, the higher the probability that the ant will choose to move towards that node[#AntQAP##1###,#AntsAndStigmergy##1###,#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. The probability with which ants choose links to neighbouring nodes are defined and discussed in the next section.

Due to ants choosing links to node based on a probability, it is still possible for the ant to choose a random link towards an other node. Thus the <#3880#>ACO<#3880#> algorithms are considered stochastic search procedures due to the ants ability to choose links randomly when exploring the search space [#ACOSurvey##1###,#ImpACOComplex##1###].

The pheromone trail was initially developed with only one colony in mind [#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. In research done by Tiwari et al.[#ACOLargeProblem##1###] pheromones in multiple colonies are considered. The basic principle of how pheromones are used by the ants stays the same, but the meaning of the pheromone changes if an ant of another colony encounters the pheromone trail[#AntQAP##1###,#AntsAndStigmergy##1###,#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. The ant will not follow or even consider the pheromone trail since any pheromone encountered from other colonies repulses the ant[#ACOLargeProblem##1###]. Thus pheromones only provide positive feedback if the ant is from the same colony, otherwise the pheromone gives negative feedback, in a way warning the ant to stay away[#ACOLargeProblem##1###]. This repulsion strategy promotes exploration among the multiple colonies[#ACOLargeProblem##1###]. The probability with which a link towards a particular node is chosen by an ant forms part of the state transition rules.


Pheromone Evaporation

Initially when the pheromone concept was first implemented the ants of the colony rapidly converged on a solution[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. The search space was not adequately explored and the produced solution was of local optima[#AntsAndStigmergy##1###]. To combat this premature convergence and force the ants to explore the search space more, the concept of pheromone evaporation was introduced[#AntIntroTrends##1###,#AntSurvey##1###].

Real pheromones used by ants to mark a particular link to a node is not permanent and over time the strength of the pheromone deteriorates until it eventually disappears[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. Pheromone deteriorating is known in the literature as pheromone evaporation[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. The pheromone will not completely evaporate as long there are ants traversing the defined link reinforcing the pheromone. The evaporation of pheromones is modelled in the <#3897#>ACO<#3897#> by equation #eq:pheromoneevapuration#3898>[#AntIntroTrends##1###,#AntSurvey##1###]:

#math76#
#tex2html_wrap_indisplay15317# (5.1)

The constant #tex2html_wrap_inline15319# mathend000# defines the rate at which the pheromone evaporates. If #tex2html_wrap_inline15321# mathend000# the pheromone completely evaporates every iteration. With no pheromone on a link towards a node the ants take no knowledge gained from the previous iteration into account and therefore select a link randomly [#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###,#AntsAndStigmergy##1###]. Thus, the amount of exploration done by the algorithm can be controlled by the constant #tex2html_wrap_inline15323# mathend000# [#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###,#AntsAndStigmergy##1###].

Equation #eq:pheromoneevapuration#3907> was first introduced in the <#3908#>AS<#3908#> [#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###,#AntSurvey##1###]. Most subsequent algorithms that are a form of the <#3910#>ACO<#3910#> class of algorithms also use the concept of pheromone evaporation, but they either use the standard equation or develop their own variant [#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###,#AntsAndStigmergy##1###].

A more aggressive form of pheromone evaporation is added to the <#3912#>AS<#3912#> discussed in the research done by Gambardella et. al[#AntQAP##1###]. The more aggressive form works beside the already present pheromone evaporation, but this form seeks to add an additional search phase called diversification[#AntQAP##1###]. The aim of the diversification phase is to lead the algorithm into another direction of the search space[#AntQAP##1###]. This is done in an attempt to avoid local minima and stagnation[#AntQAP##1###].

In the ant system developed by Gambardella et. al. the algorithm continually monitors the current best solution and keeps a history of recent best solutions[#AntQAP##1###]. If the algorithm starts to notice that solutions are cycling or that the current best solution has not changed for a certain number of iterations, the algorithm activates the diversification phase[#AntQAP##1###]. In this phase the algorithm is forced to re-search the search space to create new solutions, as it cannot rely on previous historical information provided by the pheromone trails[#AntQAP##1###].


State Transition Rules

The intention for this section is not to give an exhaustive survey of different transition rules in the literature. Therefore, only the first transition rule that was developed is discussed, since most of the other rules can be considered derivatives of the first.

As discussed previously, the ants select which link to follow towards a node based on a probability. This probability is also known as the transition probability and is formulated by equation~#eq:ASprobability#3924>.

#math77#
#tex2html_wrap_indisplay15326# (5.2)

The transition probability is used by individual ants of the <#3942#>AS<#3942#> algorithm [#AntQAP##1###,#FundamentalSwarm##1###]. An ant #tex2html_wrap_inline15328# mathend000# uses this equation to decide with what probability it will move from node #tex2html_wrap_inline15330# mathend000# to node #tex2html_wrap_inline15332# mathend000# [#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###,#ACOLargeProblem##1###]. #tex2html_wrap_inline15334# mathend000# is the amount of pheromone on the link between nodes #tex2html_wrap_inline15336# mathend000# and #tex2html_wrap_inline15338# mathend000# [#AntsAndStigmergy##1###,#ACOLargeProblem##1###]. Heuristic information is incorporated into the equation through the symbol #tex2html_wrap_inline15340# mathend000#, which is the desirability of the link from node #tex2html_wrap_inline15342# mathend000# to node #tex2html_wrap_inline15344# mathend000# as evaluated by a heuristic function [#AntsAndStigmergy##1###,#ACOLargeProblem##1###].

Each ant starting at a source node moves from one node to another based on the defined transition probaility until the ant reaches the final node.

Through the use of parameters #tex2html_wrap_inline15346# mathend000# to represent pheromone intensity and #tex2html_wrap_inline15348# mathend000# to represent heuristic information the algorithm is able to achieve a good balance between exploration and exploitation when #math78##tex2html_wrap_inline15350# mathend000# [#ACOLargeProblem##1###,#AntQAP##1###]. When #math79##tex2html_wrap_inline15352# mathend000# no pheromone is taken into account; hence, any history that the algorithm has on the link between node #tex2html_wrap_inline15354# mathend000# and node #tex2html_wrap_inline15356# mathend000# is neglected and the algorithm degrades to a stochastic greedy search procedure. If #tex2html_wrap_inline15358# mathend000# then the algorithm does not take into account the amount of desirability of the link between node #tex2html_wrap_inline15360# mathend000# and node #tex2html_wrap_inline15362# mathend000# as dictated by the problem-specific heuristic function.

The set #math80##tex2html_wrap_inline15364# mathend000# contains all the valid neighbourhood moves ant #tex2html_wrap_inline15366# mathend000# is allowed to make when moving from node #tex2html_wrap_inline15368# mathend000# to node #tex2html_wrap_inline15370# mathend000#. A tabu list is kept by each ant to trim the set of moves already performed previously, and thus cycling is prevented.

The interested reader that requires more information about state transition rules is directed to the survey by Engelbrecht[#FundamentalSwarm##1###].

Pheromone Update

Pheromones start to evaporate over time, and so the link marked by a pheromone trail becomes less attractive to the ants. Therefore, a path that represents a good solution needs its pheromone trail to be continuously reinforced. Certian rules govern when and by how much pheromones are reinforced.

Most of the variants that have been developed differ in what pheromone update rules they employ. In the literature pheromone update rules are classified into two groups [#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. One group is called the global update rule. The other group is called the iteration-based or local update rule[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###].

The first local pheromone update rule was first introduced in the <#3954#>AS<#3954#> algorithm [#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###,#AntSurvey##1###,#AntsAndStigmergy##1###]. The ants would retrace their path after each iteration, depositing pheromones on each link that makes the complete path. The following equation is used to update the pheromone:

#tex2html_wrap_indisplay15372# #tex2html_wrap_indisplay15373# (5.3)
where;SPMnbsp;#tex2html_wrap_indisplay15374# #tex2html_wrap_indisplay15375# ;SPMnbsp;;SPMnbsp;;SPMnbsp;

In equation~#eq:pheromonedeposit#3967> #math81##tex2html_wrap_inline15377# mathend000# represents the amount of pheromone that will be on the link for the next time step #tex2html_wrap_inline15379# mathend000#. #tex2html_wrap_inline15381# mathend000# represents the amount of pheromone currently on the link #tex2html_wrap_inline15383# mathend000#. #math82##tex2html_wrap_inline15385# mathend000# is the actual amount of pheromone that needs to be added to the current pheromone #tex2html_wrap_inline15387# mathend000#.

Pheromone update rules that are in the global update group only allow the pheromone trail of the path representing the best found solution since the first iteration to be updated [#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. Thus the global rule favours intensification where the algorithm exploits the global knowledge gained by the ants to find a better solution. By updating a pheromone the concentration of the particular pheromone is reinforced.

<#3973#>ACS<#3973#> was the first to use both the global update rule and local update rule together[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. By using both types of rules the algorithm is able to efficiently exploit the history provided by the pheromones[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. The global update rule used by the <#3976#>ACS<#3976#> is formulated in the following equation[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]:

#tex2html_wrap_indisplay15388# #tex2html_wrap_indisplay15389# (5.4)
where;SPMnbsp;#tex2html_wrap_indisplay15390# #tex2html_wrap_indisplay15391#if #math83##tex2html_wrap_inline15393# mathend000##tex2html_wrap_indisplay15394#otherwise#tex2html_wrap_indisplay15395# ;SPMnbsp;;SPMnbsp;;SPMnbsp;

The parameter #tex2html_wrap_inline15397# mathend000# represents the best/shortest path found so far by the algorithm[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. #tex2html_wrap_inline15399# mathend000# is the variable that controls the rate of evaporation. #math84##tex2html_wrap_inline15401# mathend000# is the amount of pheromone at the current time step #tex2html_wrap_inline15403# mathend000# for the link #tex2html_wrap_inline15405# mathend000#.

By using the global update rule the algorithm is able to direct the search more, which is to say the algorithm exploits the search space more. Exploitation is achieved since the best path is continually used in the update of the pheromone as can be observed in equation~#eq:pheromoneupdate#3994>[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###,#FundamentalSwarm##1###].

As can been seen in the following equation, the <#3996#>ACS<#3996#> uses a slight variant of the local update rule first used in <#3997#>AS<#3997#>[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]:

#math85#
#tex2html_wrap_indisplay15407# (5.5)

In the above equation #tex2html_wrap_inline15409# mathend000# is a small constant and #math86##tex2html_wrap_inline15411# mathend000# is the constant that defines the rate of evaporation[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###]. With the local update rule, the algorithm is able to explore more. The path constructed by the individual ant is used to update the pheromone and no information from the best path found in the colony is incorporated, as with equation~#eq:pheromoneupdate#4004>[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###,#FundamentalSwarm##1###].

The <#4006#>MMAS<#4006#> algorithm as discussed also improves on the <#4007#>AS<#4007#>. The global update rule used by <#4008#>AS<#4008#> has a disadvantage in the sense that the search might concentrate too quickly on a particular good solution (the global best path)[#FundamentalSwarm##1###]. <#4010#>MMAS<#4010#> address this disadvantage by using the global update rule on an iteration basis[#FundamentalSwarm##1###].

When considering only on an per iteration basis, the best path found by the algorithm can differ from one iteration to the next[#FundamentalSwarm##1###]. Therefore the <#4013#>MMAS<#4013#> a different path will be updating with the global update rule each iteration[#FundamentalSwarm##1###]. Using this approach the algorithm is allowed to explore the search space more[#FundamentalSwarm##1###].

Another short coming of the <#4016#>AS<#4016#> is that pheromone concentrations can become extremely high leading to less exploration by the algorithm[#FundamentalSwarm##1###]. The <#4018#>MMAS<#4018#> algorithm addresses this short coming by enforcing an maximum and minimum amount of pheromone that can exist on a path[#FundamentalSwarm##1###]. By defining a maximum the algorithm is prevents the algorithm from settling on one particular solution i.e, stagnation[#FundamentalSwarm##1###]. On the other hand, defining a minimum on all possible links between nodes ensures that links will be continiously considered for possible inclusion into a solution[#FundamentalSwarm##1###]. In addition to providing boundaries for the pheromones <#4022#>MMAS<#4022#> also uses a smoothing strategy to even out the difference between high and low pheromones[#FundamentalSwarm##1###].

Flow of the Algorithm

In this section the process the <#4025#>AS<#4025#> algorithm uses to explore the search space is described using algorithm~#alg:ACO#4026> as a reference point.
#algorithm4027#


#algorithm4045#

The <#4065#>ACO<#4065#> algorithm initialises by creating a set population of ants and placing them on random starting nodes as well as initialising the pheromones to starting values as can be observed from algorithm~#alg:ACO#4066>, lines 1 -- 3. The main purpose of the ant is to explore the search space and to ultimately produce a solution that might be optimal. The ant explores the search space by performing a series of moves from one node to another. Each move is a link that is added to the path. This process can be seen in lines 5 -- 10.

The ant selects which node to move to next based on a probability. The probability is calculated taking into account the amount of pheromone that is on the current link representing the movement from the current node to the next node[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###,#FundamentalSwarm##1###]. This decision process can be seen to occur in line 8.

As the ant moves it records each link between the nodes it traverses until it reaches the final node. All the links the ant has traversed represent a path taken through the search space[#CompuIntelligenceIntro##1###,#FundamentalSwarm##1###]. Thus, as the ant is moving it is actively building an optimal solution.

Before the ant deposits pheromone on the links it traversed to construct its solution, the pheromones first need to be decayed. This is why in lines 14 -- 16, the algorithm traverses all links that contain pheromones and reduces the amount of pheromones by applying equation~#eq:pheromoneevapuration#4069>.

Once an ant has constructed a path and the pheromone evaporation has occurred, the ant is ready to inform the rest of the ants of what movements it made to construct its solution. The ant needs to share this movement information in order for the rest of the colony to know which movements worked well and which did not. The ant therefore needs to signal the other ants, which is accomplished with pheromones. Therefore, in the next phase of the algorithm, pheromones are deposited on all the links that make up the path the particular ant constructed. In the algorithm pheromones are deposited on lines 17 -- 22 in algorithm~#alg:ACO1#4070>.

After all the ants have deposited pheromones on all the links represented by each individual ant's constructed solution, the algorithm is ready to continue to its next iteration. This process occurs until some defined stopping criterion occurs.

ACO on the FAP

ACO has been applied to a wide number of problems and has produced good results. As discussed in chapter~#chpt:fap#4072>, the <#4073#>FAP<#4073#> can be modelled as a graph and therefore the <#4074#>ACO<#4074#> has also been applied to it.

When using the <#4075#>ACO<#4075#> algorithm on the <#4076#>FAP<#4076#> the ants need to construct a path that represents a frequency plan and has low interference. With the <#4077#>ACO<#4077#>, a node is a cell that has a unique set of channels assigned to it. Thus the same cell may exist in the search space, but will have a different set of channels assigned to it, and will therefore represent an entirely different node to the <#4078#>ACO<#4078#>.

As an ant moves in the frequency planning domain, it is actually moving between two cells that are said to interfere. The interference between two cells occurs as a consequence of the channels that have been assigned to them.

As an ant completes a movement from one cell to another, i.e. it assigns channels to the cell, it measures the interference that occurs due to the assignment. The measured interference information is incorporated into the pheromone, which the ant will deposit on the link between the two cells.

An optimal frequency plan would therefore be a path through all the interfering cells marked with a high dosage of pheromone. As discussed in the previous sections, the pheromone indicates the desirability of a particular path. In the <#4079#>FAP<#4079#>, a desirable path would be one where interference is low; thus a path with a high dosage of pheromones would be the frequency plan with the lowest interference found by the algorithm.

When analysing the basic <#4080#>ACO<#4080#> algorithm~#alg:ACO#4081> one can identify the following possible disadvantages if the algorithm were applied to the <#4082#>FAP<#4082#>:

Memory Usage

--- The algorithm requires a fair amount of memory. The memory is used to keep track of each permutation of a particular cell and its allocated frequencies until the pheromone that links to the cell has decayed enough to be discarded. As an ant moves from one cell to another, it might not select the previous cell (due to probability) to move to, but rather generates an entirely new cell to move towards. This newly generated cell would then be linked to the previous cell, and therefore the algorithm needs to keep track of the pheromone on that link until it has completely been decayed away. The algorithm needs to keep track of these pheromones on the links even if the new link to the generated cell is not even close to optimal and has very high interference.

Building a solution

--- The <#4085#>ACO<#4085#> builds an optimal solution. Therefore, early decisions made by the ants still influence the plan later for better or for worse. A good decision might seem to be good early on, but later the algorithm might be better off with a slightly worse decision. In the <#4087#>FAP<#4087#>, a cell can have multiple interfering cells, but a particular ant only knows about one link between two cells and not about the other interfering cells. Thus an ant will find the optimal path on the first interfering link between two cells, in other words it will optimise the channels allocated to these cells so that interference is low. The first interfering link is now optimised, and subsequent ants will reinforce this channel allocation since the interference is low. When the ants later reach the other cells that also interfere with the first cell that has been optimised, they will have difficulty changing the assignments that have already been made, since the pheromone representing that assignment is too strong to disregard.

The above disadvantages have only been identified by critically evaluating the algorithm as a possible point of interest to produce an optimal solution for the <#4088#>FAP<#4088#> for this dissertation. Even with these disadvantages the <#4089#>ACO<#4089#> has achieved success in producing high quality optimal solutions for the <#4090#>FAP<#4090#>.

In research conducted by Luna et al.[#ACOvsEA##1###] an <#4092#>ACO<#4092#> algorithm was applied to a custom cellular network instance. This network instance had 711 sectors with 2 612 transceivers, which needed to be assigned frequencies. For their particular network, only 18 channels were available for assignment. The channels started at 134 and ended at 151[#ACOvsEA##1###].

The authors presented two versions of the algorithm. The first version used no heuristic information (henceforth referred to as <#4094#>ACO<#4094#>*) and the other version used heuristic information to update the pheromone laid done by the artificial ants[#ACOvsEA##1###].

With regard to the heuristic updating of the pheromone trails, the authors opted to increase the pheromone by some magnitude[#ACOvsEA##1###]. This magnitude was handtuned to be 100. The heuristic only increases a certain path's pheromone if the frequencies assigned to the transceivers represented by this path differ enough so as to not cause significant interference[#ACOvsEA##1###]. Thus, the heuristic aims to amplify good choices made previously by the algorithm for the next iteration of the algorithm.

In table~#tab:ACO#4098> the results abtained by Lune et. al [#ACOvsEA##1###] are presented. By evaluating the results obtained, one can clearly see that the <#4100#>ACO<#4100#> version that incorporates heuristic information to reinforce pheromone trails outperforms the version that does not [#ACOvsEA##1###]. The values depicted in the table represent the amount of interference that will result if the plan is used in the network[#ACOvsEA##1###].

<#15428#>Table<#15428#> 5.1: <#15429#>ACO and <#4109#>ACO<#4109#>* on custom GSM <#4110#>FAP<#4110#> benchmark[#ACOvsEA##1###]<#15429#>
Time limit ACO* ACO

Artificial Bee Colony (ABC) Algorithm

Introduction

The <#4117#>ABC<#4117#> algorithm is the most recently presented algorithm in the literature discussed in this chapter[#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCLeafConstrained##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###]. The algorithm was first proposed by Karaboga in 2005 who wanted to mimic the foraging behaviour exhibited by bees [#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCLeafConstrained##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###]. Like ants, bees need to gather food to support the colony. To understand how the <#4120#>ABC<#4120#> algorithm tries to mimic the foraging behaviour of bees, this behaviour of real bees needs to be described first[#ABCCompareStudy##1###].

In a bee colony there are numerous bees, each with a specific role that dictates what actions a bee can perform. There are bees that protect the queen, maintain the colony, scout for resources and gather food, i.e the worker bees. The most important bees for foraging are those that scout and gather food[#ABCCompareStudy##1###].

The scout bees are sent out and as their role implies, are responsible for exploring the surroundings of the hive to find suitable food sources[#ABCCompareStudy##1###]. If a scout bee has found a food source it needs to return to the colony to share the information with the worker bees[#ABCCompareStudy##1###]. When the bee enters the colony it needs to communicate to the other bees by using some form of stigmergy (see section~#sec:stigmergy#4125>)[#ABCCompareStudy##1###].

The scout bee accomplishes this communication by performing a dance known as the waggle dance in the colony for all the bees to see[#ABCCompareStudy##1###]. This is not a dance as in the traditional sense, since through certain movements the bee is able to communicate a variety of characteristics about the food source including[#ABCCompareStudy##1###]:

  • How far the food source is from the colony
  • Quality of the food source
  • Path towards the food source

It can be concluded that foraging bees use sematectonic stigmergy (discussed in section~#sec:stigmergy#4133>). This is deduced from the dance which is a physical form of communication.

The dance is observed by onlooker worker bees [#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCImageEnhancement##1###]. These onlooker bees are initially unemployed in the colony [#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCImageEnhancement##1###]. Once the information of the scout has been transferred to the onlooker bees, the onlooker bees become employed bees[#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCImageEnhancement##1###]. They become employed bees when they operate on a particular food source to gather food[#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCImageEnhancement##1###]. Thus it is the job of the worker bees to exploit the information provided by the exploration done by the scout bees [#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###].

Worker bees gather food from the designated food source, until the food source reaches a certain quantity with regard to nectar content [#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###]. Each time the bee returns to the colony it evaluates the current food source versus other food sources discovered [#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###]. If a better food source is found, the bee abandons the previous source and starts gathering food from the new source [#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###]. On the other hand, if the food source has been exhausted, meaning there is no more nectar content to gather, the bee returns to the colony and becomes ``unemployed'' [#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###].

In the <#4148#>ABC<#4148#> algorithm, possible solutions are considered to be food sources[#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###]. Each food source has an employed bee associated with it. Onlooker bees either wait for new food sources to be communicated to them or become employed bees by moving to another, more attractive food source [#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###].

A food source might be more attractive to a bee because its defined nectar content is more than that of the current food source the bee is operating on[#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###]. The nectar content of a food source can be considered to be the fitness, which is determined using the fitness function of the specific problem domain[#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###].

As with real honey bees, a waggle dance is performed to all the onlooker bees by employed bees that provide information on the nectar amount (fitness value) that they represent [#ABCReconfigDistro##1###,#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCImageEnhancement##1###]. The onlooker bees choose food sources depending on the nectar amount [#ABCReconfigDistro##1###,#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCImageEnhancement##1###]; therefore as the nectar amount of a food source increases, the probability that more onlooker bees will choose the source increases [#ABCReconfigDistro##1###,#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCImageEnhancement##1###]. How and what affects the probability is discussed in the next subsection.

Bees can transition to different roles depending on their situation [#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###]. An onlooker bee becomes employed when assigned to a food source and an employed bee can become a scout if its initial food source becomes exhausted [#ABCImageEnhancement##1###,#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCReconfigDistro##1###]. Note that not all employed bees of a food source become scouts; only the first employed bee of a food source transitions to a scout [#ABCImageEnhancement##1###,#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCReconfigDistro##1###]. Scout bees are sent to randomly generated food sources [#ABCImageEnhancement##1###,#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCReconfigDistro##1###].

The more onlooker bees a food source attracts, the more the neighbourhood will be explored since the onlooker bees move to the food source and choose an immediate neighbouring food source to be employed upon [#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###]. Thus, this can be considered exploitation and the algorithm is therefore performing a local search[#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCReconfigDistro##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###]. Finally, the number of onlooker bees a food source has also indicates its desirability. A very good solution will have the majority of onlooker bees choosing it and searching for nearby better food sources [#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCReconfigDistro##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###]. More bees are lured towards a particular food source due to the high nectar amount that has been communicated to them by other employed bees[#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCReconfigDistro##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###].

When a food source is abandoned, the previous bee that occupied the food source transitions to a scout bee [#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###]. The scout bee is responsible for replacing the abandoned food source by finding a new one, and a new food source is generated and communicated back to the colony[#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCImageEnhancement##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###]. The generation of food sources is discussed in the next subsection.

Karaboga was not the first to base an algorithm on the above foraging behaviour. Other bee foraging inspired algorithms have been developed such as the BeeHive algorithm, <#4167#>BCO<#4167#> and <#4168#>BSO<#4168#> [#BCO##1###,#HybridABCClustering##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###].

The BeeHive algorithm is based on the dance communication used inside the colony of bees. In <#4170#>BCO<#4170#> solutions are randomly generated and assigned to bees[#HybridABCClustering##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###]. Finally, <#4172#>BSO<#4172#> solutions are iteratively constructed by forager (worker) bees and the best solution is communicated to the rest of the colony by performing a dance[#HybridABCClustering##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###].

Another bee algorithm is the <#4174#>VBA<#4174#> which, like the previous algorithms, is also based on the foraging behaviour of bees, but it differs in that it is not designed for combinatorial problems [#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###]. Instead the <#4176#>VBA<#4176#> is a variant of the standard <#4177#>ABC<#4177#> algorithm which is designed for numerical function optimisation [#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###]. In <#4179#>VBA<#4179#> bees move around in the search space communicating to each other any target nectar food sources that are found[#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###]. Good food sources are function evaluations of particular coordinates in the numerical search space which produce low function evaluation values in the case of minimisation[#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###].

Karaboga developed the <#4182#>ABC<#4182#> algorithm based on the previous research done on bee colony optimisation and the above algorithms. The <#4183#>ABC<#4183#> algorithm is designed to be a multivariable optimisation algorithm and has to date been applied to the job scheduling problem, clustering [#HybridABCClustering##1###], neural network training and reconfiguration of distribution networks [#ABCReconfigDistro##1###]. Due to the nature of the algorithm being similar to that of the <#4186#>ACO<#4186#>, the <#4187#>ABC<#4187#> algorithm will most likely also be applied to a whole host of other of problems.

ABC Algorithm Characteristics

Various characteristics of the <#4189#>ABC<#4189#> algorithm define the algorithm and make it unique. The first characteristic is how food sources are handled in the algorithm. The second is how information is communicated to the colony.


Food Sources

As discussed previously, food sources represent solutions to the problem the <#4192#>ABC<#4192#> algorithm is being applied to. When the algorithm starts, there are no defined food sources for the bees to evaluate and report on, and therefore initially a finite number of food sources are randomly generated[#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCFusionGrid##1###]. Since each food source needs an employed bee to evaluate the nectar amount of the source, the parameter that defines the number of food sources also defines the number of employed bees[#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCLeafConstrained##1###].

Employed bees evaluate these food sources by determining their nectar amount[#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCLeafConstrained##1###]. The nectar amount is directly related to the fitness value calculated using a domain specific cost function[#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCReconfigDistro##1###]. After the amount is determined the employed bee advertises the food source to the colony by performing the waggle dance.

Onlooker bees witness a number of dances from a variety of employed bees[#ABCFusionGrid##1###,#BeeJobShop##1###]. They therefore need to select a food source that is the most attractive while maintaining some diversity in the pool of solutions. Thus, an onlooker bee selects a food source based on a probability function which is formulated in equation #eq:beeProbability#4198>[#ABCCompareStudy##1###]:

#math87#
#tex2html_wrap_indisplay15436# (5.6)

The parameter #tex2html_wrap_inline15438# mathend000# is the #tex2html_wrap_inline15440# mathend000#th food source under consideration by the onlooker bee. The #tex2html_wrap_inline15442# mathend000# parameter represents the value of the cost function and is directly related to the nectar amount of food source #tex2html_wrap_inline15444# mathend000#. The parameter SN is the maximum amount of food source and hence the maximum employed onlooker bees [#ABCCompareStudy##1###].

In algorithm~#alg:ABC#4209> the waggle dance can be seen being applied in line 11 where the probability of all the employed bee' solutions are calculated using equation~#eq:beeProbability#4210>. From lines 12 -- 16 the onlooker bees evaluate the solutions of the employed bees based on the probability #tex2html_wrap_inline15446# mathend000# that was calculated. #tex2html_wrap_inline15448# mathend000# can be seen as the rating of the waggle dance that was performed by the employed bee.


Employed and Onlooker Bees

As previously outlined, when recruited onlooker bees reach the advertised food source that is stored in memory, they do not occupy the same food source[#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###]. Instead the bees explore the immediate neighbourhood of the food source that was communicated to them[#BeeJobShop##1###,#ABCFusionGrid##1###,#ABCReconfigDistro##1###]. They seek to find a food source that improves on the previous one[#BeeJobShop##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###]. Equation #eq:beeGenerate#4216> is used by the bees to generate new food sources in the neighbourhood of food source #tex2html_wrap_inline15451# mathend000#[#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCFusionGrid##1###].

#math88#
#tex2html_wrap_indisplay15453# (5.7)

The subscripts #math89##tex2html_wrap_inline15455# mathend000# and #math90##tex2html_wrap_inline15457# mathend000# are indices which are randomly chosen. #tex2html_wrap_inline15459# mathend000# is the maximum dimensionality of the vector a solution represents. The index #tex2html_wrap_inline15461# mathend000# has a constraint tied to it -- whatever value is randomly assigned to #tex2html_wrap_inline15463# mathend000# must differ from the value #tex2html_wrap_inline15465# mathend000#. The position of the new food source in the neighbourhood of #tex2html_wrap_inline15467# mathend000# is controlled by the #tex2html_wrap_inline15469# mathend000# parameter, which is a bounded random value between #tex2html_wrap_inline15471# mathend000#.

From equation #eq:beeGenerate#4229> it can be concluded that the randomness of the food source position decreases as the difference between #math91##tex2html_wrap_inline15473# mathend000# decreases. Thus, as the algorithm moves closer to an optimal solution the finer grained the search process of the algorithm becomes[#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###,#ABCImageEnhancement##1###].

After a new solution #tex2html_wrap_inline15475# mathend000# is produced, the bee takes the new and old solutions from memory to compare their respective nectar contents. If the new solution is found to have higher quality nectar, the bee replaces the old solution in memory with the new solution[#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCReconfigDistro##1###]. Otherwise, the bee abandons the new solution and keeps the old solution in memory[#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###]. Thus, the bee seeks to always move towards a better solution and therefore uses a greedy selection process[#ABCLeafConstrained##1###,#ABCReconfigDistro##1###].

One of the problems with the above approach is that little information about the food source is used in generating a neighbouring food source. In the research by Singh [#ABCLeafConstrained##1###] a slight variation is proposed to generating food source neighbours by using more global information. The author adds a constraint to the algorithm that all neighbouring solutions generated by employed bees must be unique.

When an employed bee generates a neighbour and an identical solution already exists in the system, a collision is said to have occurred. A collision is solved by letting the employed bee transition to a scout bee so that a completely random solution can be generated [#ABCLeafConstrained##1###]. Scout and onlooker bee generated solutions are not checked if they collide with other solutions in the system since the aim for them is exploring and not exploiting as with employed bees[#BeeJobShop##1###,#ABCCompareStudy##1###].

Scout Bee

The artificial bees are modelled on the behaviour of real bees. Thus an employed bee can also abandon certain food sources when it has outlived its usefulness. Abandonment of a food source can occur for the following reasons[#BeeJobShop##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###,#ABCImageEnhancement##1###]:
  • The employed bee has reached the maximum allowed cycles to improve the nectar amount. The maximum cycles spent on a food source allow the algorithm to avoid local optima[#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###,#ABCImageEnhancement##1###].
  • The bee cannot improve the search represented by the food source any further[#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###,#ABCImageEnhancement##1###].
When a food source in the algorithm is abandoned, it needs to be replaced by a new food source[#BeeJobShop##1###,#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCImageEnhancement##1###]. Note that a food source is not abandoned when it represents the globally best found solution. An employed bee transitions to a new role when it abandons a food source from an employed bee to a scout bee[#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCNumericalOptimization##1###,#ABCImageEnhancement##1###].

It is the responsibility of the scout bee to replace the abandoned food source with a new randomly generated one[#BeeJobShop##1###,#ABCCompareStudy##1###,#ABCImageEnhancement##1###]. The scout bee uses equation #eq:scoutGenerate#4250> to produce a new food source that will replace food source #tex2html_wrap_inline15478# mathend000#.

#math92#
#tex2html_wrap_indisplay15480# (5.8)

The subscript #tex2html_wrap_inline15482# mathend000# represents the minimum value of #tex2html_wrap_inline15484# mathend000# and the subscript #tex2html_wrap_inline15486# mathend000# represents the maximum value of #tex2html_wrap_inline15488# mathend000#.

In research done by Gómez-Iglesias et al. [#ABCFusionGrid##1###] an extension is made to the scout bees. The scout bee individuals are divided into two types of bees, namely rovers and cubs bees[#ABCFusionGrid##1###].
